Ash'ari Defense: Divine Will and Opposites
Ash'ari argues divine will only applies to what occurs, not to opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians defend the coherence of divine will in relation to opposite events.

Core Idea
Divine will attaches to acts as events, not as opposites.

The Ash'ari position on divine will faces an apparent difficulty: if God wills both belief and unbelief, does He not will two opposites? This passage presents a sophisticated response, distinguishing between will and wish, and clarifying that eternal will relates to acts only qua their occurrence as renewed events. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The objection claims that willing two opposite acts entails willing two opposites. Grasp why this seems problematic.
The objector assumes that willing a will includes willing its object.
2
Distinguish will from wish
The passage first denies two wills for opposites, separating actual volition from mere desire. Note the two meanings of 'will'.
The eternal will can mean wish/desire or volition.
3
Follow the analogical reasoning
The response uses analogies of will for power and knowledge of others' beliefs to show that willing a capacity does not entail willing its object.
God's knowledge of Zayd's knowledge does not make Him a believer in that knowledge.
4
Grasp the secret and the alternative formulation
The eternal will's attachment is to renewal, not opposition. The alternative view combines will for one event and dislike for the other.
Quranic verses support this: God intends ease, not hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: لسنا نسلم أولا أن هاهنا إرادتين للضدين، بل هو ما وقع في معلوم الرب تعالى أن يقع فهو المراد وصاحبه المريد وما علم أنه لا يقع فليس مرادًا وصاحبه المتمني، ويجوز تعلق الإرادة القديمة بمعنيين؛ أحدهما تمن وشهوة، والثاني إرادة.
ثم قالوا: لِمَ قلتم: إن إرادة الإرادتين تقتضي إرادة المرادين حتى يلزم الجمع بين الضدين؟ فإنه تعالى إنما أراد إرادتيهما من حيث وجودهما وتجددهما لا من حيث مراديهما، وهو كإرادته للقدرة لا تكون إرادة للمقدور، وقدرته على الإرادة لا تكون قدرة على المواد على أصلكم كعلمه بعلم زيد وظن عمرو وشك خالد وجهل بكر لا يكون اعتقادًا لمعتقداتهم حتى يوصف بمتعلقات صفاتهم.
والسر في ذلك أن الإرادة القديمة لم تتعلق إلا بوجه واحد وهو المتجدد من حيث هو حادث متجدد متخصص بالوجود دون العدم ووقت دون وقت، والإرادتان تشتركان في التجدد فتنتسبان إليها من جهة التجدد والتخصص وهما هذا الوجه ليسا ضدين فلم تتعلق الإرادة بالضدين ولو قيل: تتعلق الإرادة من بالإرادتين جميعًا من حيث الوقوع والتجدد وبأحد المرادين وهو الواقع منهما في المعلوم، وتتعلق بعدم وقوع الآخر فهي إرادة لوقوع أحدهما وكراهة لوقوع الآخر كان ذلك أيضا صواباً، قال الله تعالى: {يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ} [البقرة:185] أي: يريد خلاف العسر، كما قال: {أتنبئون الله بما لا يعلم} [يونس:18] أي بما يعلم خلافه حتى لا يكون النفي داخلا في الإطلاقات.
They said: We do not concede first that there are two wills for the two opposites. Rather, that which occurs in the knowledge of the Lord, Exalted, that it will occur, it is the object of will and its possessor is the willer; and that which He knows will not occur, it is not an object of will and its possessor is the one who wishes. And it is permissible for the eternal will to be attached to two meanings: one of them is wish and desire, and the second is will. Then they said: Why did you say that the will for the two wills necessitates will for the two objects of will, so that combination of opposites becomes necessary? For He, Exalted, only willed their two wills from the aspect of their existence and their renewal, not from the aspect of their objects of will. And it is like His will for power: it is not a will for the thing powered, and His power over will is not power over the matters according to your principle, like His knowledge of Zayd's knowledge, Amr's opinion, Khalid's doubt, and Bakr's ignorance: it does not become belief in their beliefs so that He is described by the objects of their attributes. And the secret in that is that the eternal will was attached only to one aspect, namely the renewed from the aspect that it is a renewed event specifically determined by existence not non-existence, and by one time not another. The two wills share in renewal, so they relate to it from the aspect of renewal and determination. They, in this aspect, are not opposites, so the will was not attached to opposites. And if it were said: The will is attached to the two wills both together from the aspect of occurrence and renewal, and to one of the two objects of will, namely the one that occurs in knowledge, and it is attached to the non-occurrence of the other, so it is a will for the occurrence of one and a dislike (karaha) for the occurrence of the other, that too would be correct. Allah Most High said: {Allah intends for you ease and does not intend for you hardship} (al-Baqarah 2:185), meaning He intends the opposite of hardship, as He said: {Do you inform Allah of what He does not know?} (Yunus 10:18), meaning of what He knows the opposite of, so that negation is not included in absolute expressions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an Ash'ari defense against the objection that divine will cannot encompass two opposites. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, the Ash'ari states that there are not two wills for the two opposites (like belief and disbelief) because only what God knows will occur is willed; what He knows will not occur is merely wished for, not willed. The eternal will can be understood in two senses: wish/desire (tamanni wa shahwa) and volition (irada). Second, the objector asks: why does willing the two wills not necessitate willing both objects of will (i.e., both opposites)? The response is that God wills the two wills from the aspect of their existence and renewal (tajaddud), not from the aspect of their specific objects. This is analogous to His will for power: He wills the power, not necessarily the thing powered; likewise, His power over will does not extend to the objects of will. Another analogy: God knows Zayd's knowledge, Amr's opinion, etc., but that does not mean He holds those beliefs. Third, the 'secret' (al-sirr) is that the eternal will attaches only to one aspect: the act as a renewed, temporally determined event—not to its being one opposite or the other. Since both wills share renewal, the will relates to them only in that aspect, not in their opposition. An alternative formulation is that the will attaches to both wills from the aspect of occurrence, and to the actual occurring object of will while willing the non-occurrence of the other—which amounts to willing one and disliking the other. This is supported by Quranic verses: God intends ease, not hardship (meaning He intends the opposite of hardship), and the verse about informing God of what He does not know means informing Him of the opposite of what He knows, so that negation is not absolute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills two opposites, He must will them as opposites simultaneously.
Correction: God wills acts as events, not as opposites; the eternal will attaches only to the aspect of renewal.

Checkpoint

A) The specific moral quality of the act
B) The act as a renewed, temporally determined event
C) The act's status as good or evil
D) The act's being a particular opposite

Key Takeaway
The eternal will relates to acts as renewed events, not as opposites, thereby avoiding contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you deeply wished for something that did not happen, did you truly accept that it was not willed by Allah? Or did you hold a hidden resentment? How can this lesson's distinction between will and wish help you surrender to His decree with full trust?
?
This passage shows that God's will is perfectly unified, attaching only to what actually occurs. Your own will is often divided between conflicting desires. Does recognizing this contrast humble you? Will you commit to aligning your desires with what actually unfolds, even when it is painful?
?
The Ash'ari defense implies that God does not will the opposite of what occurs. For an event that you consider evil, do you believe God's will is nonetheless good and wise? Can you still say, 'Your will be done,' without doubting His justice? What is one specific area in your life where you struggle to accept His will over your own?

Mu'tazila Objection on Divine Will
Mu'tazila objection from ethical predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational argument that if God's eternal will encompasses evil, then God would be described as evil, which is repugnant.

Core Idea
Moral descriptions of willers transfer to the willed — Mu'tazila.

The Mu'tazila begin a key objection against the Ash'ari view that God's eternal will encompasses all things. They rely on a commonly accepted premise: a person who wills good is good, one who wills evil is evil, etc. From this, they argue that if God wills both good and evil, He would be described with contradictory moral attributes, which is impossible. They support this with a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the premise they present and the conclusion they draw.
The premise is about how we describe human willers.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Notice the argument: if God's will includes evil, then the attributes 'evil' and 'unjust' would apply to Him, which is repugnant.
3
Consider the Quranic evidence
They cite verse 40:31 to support that God does not will injustice. Reflect on how this fits their argument.
This is a proof text, not a rational premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: قد تقرر في العقول أن مريد الخير خير ومريد الشر شر، ومريد العدل عادل، ومريد الظلم ظالم، فلو كانت الإرادة الأزلية تتعلق بالكائنات كلها لكان الخير والشر مرادًا، فيكون المريد موصوفا بالخيرية والشرية والعدل والظلم، وذلك قبيح في حق القديم سبحانه، قال الله تعالى: {وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ} [غافر:31]
قالت الأشعرية: هذه المقدمة التي ذكرتموها من المشهورات في عادات الناس لا من الأوليات في قضايا العقول، وإنما يختص إطرادها في بعض الصور دون البعض؛ إذ لا يقال في سياق ذلك: إن مريد الجهل جاهل ومريد العلم عالم، ولا يقال مريد الطاعة مطيع ولا يقال مريد الحركة متحرك، ومريد الصلاة مصل، ثم يقال في الشاهد مريد العبادة عابد، ولا يقال في الغائب مريد العبادة عابد.
ثم السر في ذلك: أن الإرادة الحادثة قد تكون ضرورية، وقد تكون كسبية، والضرورية منها لا توصف بالخيرية والشرية والتكليفية؛ إذ لا تكليف إلا على المكتسب، وقد توصف بالخيرية والشرية الجبرية، كما يقال: الملك يريد الخير طبعا وجبرًا، والشيطان يريد الشر طبعا وجبرًا، وأما الإرادات الكسبية فتتوجه على المريد فيها وبها التكليف فيوصف بالظلم والعدل والخير والشر والطاعة والمعصية كما وصفت سائر الحركات، ثم لم يلزم من ذلك طردها في الغائب فلم يصح الاستدلال.
The Mu'tazila said: It is established in minds that the willer of good is good, and the willer of evil is evil, and the willer of justice is just, and the willer of injustice is unjust. So if the eternal will were attached to all existent things, good and evil would be willed, so the willer would be described by goodness and evilness, justice and injustice, and that is repugnant with respect to the Eternal, Exalted. Allah Most High said: {And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants} (Ghafir 40:31).
The Ash'ariyya said: This premise that you have mentioned is from common notions in the customs of people, not from primary axioms in the judgments of intellects. Its application is only specific to some cases, not to others. For it is not said in that context: 'The willer of ignorance is ignorant, and the willer of knowledge is knowledgeable'; nor is it said: 'The willer of obedience is obedient'; nor is it said: 'The willer of motion is moving, and the willer of prayer is praying.' Then it is said in the witnessed realm that the willer of worship is a worshipper, but it is not said in the unseen realm that the willer of worship is a worshipper. Then the secret in that is that the temporal will may be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acquired (kasbiyya). The necessary ones are not described by goodness, evilness, or obligatoriness, for there is no obligation except upon the acquirer. They may be described by compulsory goodness or evilness, as it is said: 'The king wills good by nature and compulsion, and the devil wills evil by nature and compulsion.' As for acquired wills, obligation attaches to the willer in and through them, so he is described by injustice, justice, good, evil, obedience, and disobedience, just as other actions are described. Then it does not follow from that that it applies in the unseen, so the inference is not valid.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya on the ethical implications of divine will. The Mu'tazila present a common-sense premise: that a person who wills good is good, one who wills evil is evil, etc. They argue that if God's eternal will encompasses all things (including evil), then God would be described with both good and evil attributes, which is repugnant. They cite a Quranic verse to support that God does not will injustice. The Ash'ari response first challenges the status of this premise, calling it a 'common notion' (mashhūrāt) rather than a primary axiom (awwaliyyāt). It does not apply universally: one is not called ignorant just for willing ignorance, or knowledgeable for willing knowledge, etc. Also, in the witnessed realm, one may say the willer of worship is a worshipper, but this is not transferred to the unseen (God). Then the Ash'ari explain the 'secret': temporal will can be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acquired (kasbiyya). Necessary wills (like natural inclinations) are not described with moral predicates; for example, a king may will good by nature, and a devil may will evil by nature, but these are compulsive, not blameworthy. Only acquired wills—those involving choice—carry moral responsibility and description. Since God's will is not acquired, the premise fails. Moreover, even if it applied in the witnessed realm, it does not necessarily apply to God (the unseen). Thus the Mu'tazila inference is invalid.

Key Terms
مريد
خير
شر
عدل
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila premise in this objection?
A) God's will is eternal and encompasses all things.
B) A person who wills good is good, and one who wills evil is evil.
C) Evil cannot exist in the world.
D) God does not will anything.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God's will includes evil, then God would be described as evil, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you secretly conclude that God is not fully in control, or do you trust that His will permits it for a wise purpose you cannot see? Which response reflects your actual heart in difficult moments?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that willing evil makes one evil. In your own life, have you ever judged someone's character solely by the negative outcomes of their actions, without considering their intention or the bigger picture? How can you apply the Ash'ari critique of this assumption to your own judgments of others?
?
This lesson shows that a direct application of human moral reasoning to God leads to an impasse. Are you willing to submit to God's revelation even when your reason cannot fully reconcile His will with your sense of justice? What would that submission cost you?

Ash'ari Response on Will Types
Ash'ari rebuttal: critique of premise and distinction of will types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari rebuttal: the Mu'tazila premise is not a primary axiom but a common notion, and moral description applies only to acquired wills, not necessary or divine wills.

Core Idea
Moral predicates apply only to acquired wills, not to God's will.

The Ash'ari respond with a multi-layered critique. First, they argue that the Mu'tazila premise is not a self-evident truth but a common cultural notion that does not hold universally. They provide examples where the premise fails. Then they reveal the deeper reason: temporal wills can be necessary or acquired. Only acquired wills incur moral responsibility and description. Since God's will is not acquired, the premise does not apply to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how they first critique the premise's epistemological status.
They call it 'mashhūrāt' (common notions) not 'awwaliyyāt' (primary axioms).
2
Examine the counterexamples
They list cases where willing something does not make the willer that thing (e.g., willing ignorance does not make one ignorant).
3
Understand the secret (al-sirr)
The Ash'ari explain that temporal wills can be necessary (compulsive) or acquired (voluntary). Only acquired wills are subject to moral description.
Necessary wills are like natural inclinations; they are not blameworthy.
4
Apply to the unseen (ghā'ib)
Even if the premise held in the witnessed world, it does not transfer to God (the unseen).
This blocks the Mu'tazila inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: قد تقرر في العقول أن مريد الخير خير ومريد الشر شر، ومريد العدل عادل، ومريد الظلم ظالم، فلو كانت الإرادة الأزلية تتعلق بالكائنات كلها لكان الخير والشر مرادًا، فيكون المريد موصوفا بالخيرية والشرية والعدل والظلم، وذلك قبيح في حق القديم سبحانه، قال الله تعالى: {وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ} [غافر:31]
قالت الأشعرية: هذه المقدمة التي ذكرتموها من المشهورات في عادات الناس لا من الأوليات في قضايا العقول، وإنما يختص إطرادها في بعض الصور دون البعض؛ إذ لا يقال في سياق ذلك: إن مريد الجهل جاهل ومريد العلم عالم، ولا يقال مريد الطاعة مطيع ولا يقال مريد الحركة متحرك، ومريد الصلاة مصل، ثم يقال في الشاهد مريد العبادة عابد، ولا يقال في الغائب مريد العبادة عابد.
ثم السر في ذلك: أن الإرادة الحادثة قد تكون ضرورية، وقد تكون كسبية، والضرورية منها لا توصف بالخيرية والشرية والتكليفية؛ إذ لا تكليف إلا على المكتسب، وقد توصف بالخيرية والشرية الجبرية، كما يقال: الملك يريد الخير طبعا وجبرًا، والشيطان يريد الشر طبعا وجبرًا، وأما الإرادات الكسبية فتتوجه على المريد فيها وبها التكليف فيوصف بالظلم والعدل والخير والشر والطاعة والمعصية كما وصفت سائر الحركات، ثم لم يلزم من ذلك طردها في الغائب فلم يصح الاستدلال.
The Mu'tazila said: It is established in minds that the willer of good is good, and the willer of evil is evil, and the willer of justice is just, and the willer of injustice is unjust. So if the eternal will were attached to all existent things, good and evil would be willed, so the willer would be described by goodness and evilness, justice and injustice, and that is repugnant with respect to the Eternal, Exalted. Allah Most High said: {And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants} (Ghafir 40:31).
The Ash'ariyya said: This premise that you have mentioned is from common notions in the customs of people, not from primary axioms in the judgments of intellects. Its application is only specific to some cases, not to others. For it is not said in that context: 'The willer of ignorance is ignorant, and the willer of knowledge is knowledgeable'; nor is it said: 'The willer of obedience is obedient'; nor is it said: 'The willer of motion is moving, and the willer of prayer is praying.' Then it is said in the witnessed realm that the willer of worship is a worshipper, but it is not said in the unseen realm that the willer of worship is a worshipper. Then the secret in that is that the temporal will may be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acquired (kasbiyya). The necessary ones are not described by goodness, evilness, or obligatoriness, for there is no obligation except upon the acquirer. They may be described by compulsory goodness or evilness, as it is said: 'The king wills good by nature and compulsion, and the devil wills evil by nature and compulsion.' As for acquired wills, obligation attaches to the willer in and through them, so he is described by injustice, justice, good, evil, obedience, and disobedience, just as other actions are described. Then it does not follow from that that it applies in the unseen, so the inference is not valid.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya on the ethical implications of divine will. The Mu'tazila present a common-sense premise: that a person who wills good is good, one who wills evil is evil, etc. They argue that if God's eternal will encompasses all things (including evil), then God would be described with both good and evil attributes, which is repugnant. They cite a Quranic verse to support that God does not will injustice. The Ash'ari response first challenges the status of this premise, calling it a 'common notion' (mashhūrāt) rather than a primary axiom (awwaliyyāt). It does not apply universally: one is not called ignorant just for willing ignorance, or knowledgeable for willing knowledge, etc. Also, in the witnessed realm, one may say the willer of worship is a worshipper, but this is not transferred to the unseen (God). Then the Ash'ari explain the 'secret': temporal will can be necessary (ḍarūriyya) or acquired (kasbiyya). Necessary wills (like natural inclinations) are not described with moral predicates; for example, a king may will good by nature, and a devil may will evil by nature, but these are compulsive, not blameworthy. Only acquired wills—those involving choice—carry moral responsibility and description. Since God's will is not acquired, the premise fails. Moreover, even if it applied in the witnessed realm, it does not necessarily apply to God (the unseen). Thus the Mu'tazila inference is invalid.

Key Terms
مشهورات
أوليات
إرادة حادثة
ضرورية
كسبية
تكليفية
جبري
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That moral descriptions transfer from humans to God universally.
Correction: The Ash'ari show that such transfer is invalid because the premise is not universally true and does not apply to necessary or divine wills.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari, why does the Mu'tazila premise not apply to God?
A) Because God's will is not a temporal will.
B) Because the premise is only a common notion and moral description applies only to acquired wills, not necessary or divine wills.
C) Because God does not will anything.
D) Because the Quran denies it.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari rebut the Mu'tazila by showing their premise is not universal and that moral predicates only apply to acquired wills, not to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold about God that are actually based on human customs rather than revelation or reason? How willing are you to admit that a cherished assumption might not apply to the Divine?
?
When you examine your own intentions, do you conflate what you naturally desire (necessary will) with what you choose to do (acquired will)? How does this distinction change the way you hold yourself morally accountable?
?
If moral descriptions only apply to acquired wills, then your salvation hinges on choices you make, not on your inclinations. Does this truth frighten you or comfort you? What will you do today to ensure your acquired wills align with obedience?

The Mu'tazila Objection: Willing Evil Implies an Evil Willer
Mu'tazila argue divine will cannot include evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's eternal will cannot include evil because that would imply moral imperfection in God.

Core Idea
Willing evil would make God evil — or so the Mu'tazila argue.

The Mu'tazila raise a powerful objection against the Ash'ariyya view that God's will encompasses all acts, including evil. They argue from a self-evident principle: a willer acquires the moral quality of what is willed. This lesson presents their objection and the Quranic proof they cite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the Arabic passage containing the Mu'tazila argument. Notice how they start with a general principle and then apply it to God.
The principle is stated in the first line.
2
Identify the core premise
The premise is that the moral quality of the willer is determined by the moral quality of what is willed. Confirm this.
3
Note the Quranic evidence
The Mu'tazila cite a Quranic verse to support their claim that God does not will injustice. Observe how they use it as proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : قد تقرر في العقول أن مريد الخير خير ومريد الشر شر، ومريد العدل عادل، ومريد الظلم ظالم، فلو كانت الإرادة الأزلية تتعلق بالكائنات كلها لكان الخير والشر مرادًا، فيكون المريد موصوفا بالخيرية والشرية والعدل والظلم، وذلك قبيح في حق القديم سبحانه، قال الله تعالى : ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ غافر: ۳۱) قالت الأشعرية : هذه المقدمة التي ذكرتموها من المشهورات في عادات الناس لا من الأوليات في قضايا العقول، وإنما يختص إطرادها في بعض الصور دون البعض ؛ إذ لا يقال في سياق ذلك : إن مريد الجهل جاهل ومريد العلم عالم، ومريد الطاعة مطيع ولا يقال مريد الحركة متحرك، ومريد الصلاة مصل، ثم يقال في الشاهد مريد العبادة عابد، ولا يقال في الغائب مريد العبادة عابد. ثم السر في ذلك : أن الإرادة الحادثة قد تكون ضرورية، وقد تكون كسبية، والضرورية منها لا توصف بالخيرية والشرية والتكليفية ؛ إذ لا تكليف إلا على المكتسب، وقد توصف بالخيرية والشرية الجبرية، كما يقال : الملك يريد الخير طبعا وجبرًا، والشيطان يريد الشر طبعا وجبرًا، وأما الإرادات الكسبية فتتوجه على المريد فيها وبها التكليف فيوصف الظلم والعدل والخير والشر والطاعة والمعصية كما وصف سائر الحركات ثم لم يلزم من ذلك طردها في الغائب فلم يصح الاستدلال.
The Mu'tazila said: It is established in intellects that the willer of good is good, and the willer of evil is evil, and the willer of justice is just, and the willer of injustice is unjust. So if the eternal will were attached to all existent things, then good and evil would be willed, and thus the willer would be described with goodness and evilness, justice and injustice, and that is ugly with respect to the Eternal, exalted. God said: 'And God does not will injustice for the servants' (Ghafir 31). The Ash'ariyya said: This premise that you mentioned is among the generally accepted notions in the customs of people, not among the self-evident matters in the judgments of intellects. Rather, its uniform application is restricted to some cases and not others; for it is not said in the context of that: that the willer of ignorance is ignorant and the willer of knowledge is knowledgeable, and the willer of obedience is obedient, and it is not said that the willer of motion is moving, and the willer of prayer is praying. Then it is said in the witnessed (world) that the willer of worship is a worshipper, but it is not said in the unseen (world) that the willer of worship is a worshipper. Then the secret in that is that temporal will may be necessary and may be acquired. The necessary among them is not described with goodness, evilness, and obligatoriness, since there is no obligation except upon the acquisitor. And it may be described with goodness and evilness by compulsion, as it is said: the king wills good by nature and compulsion, and Satan wills evil by nature and compulsion. As for the acquired wills, they are directed upon the willer in and through them the obligation, so he is described with injustice, justice, good, evil, obedience, and disobedience, just as all movements are described. Then it does not necessarily follow from that its uniform application in the unseen, so the inference is not valid.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection against the Ash'ariyya doctrine that God's eternal will includes all created acts, including evil. The Mu'tazila argue from the premise that a willer takes on the moral quality of what is willed: if God wills evil, He would be evil—impossible for God. The Ash'ariyya respond by first questioning the universal validity of this premise. They argue that it is not a self-evident truth but a culturally conditioned generalization. They provide counterexamples: willing knowledge does not make one knowledgeable, willing motion does not make one moving, etc. Then they introduce a crucial distinction: temporal will can be either necessary (uncontrollable, like natural inclinations) or acquired (freely chosen). Only acquired will carries moral responsibility; necessary will is not subject to praise or blame. Therefore, the analogy from human experience (where will reflects character) does not apply to God, whose will is eternal and not subject to these categories. The conclusion is that the Mu'tazila inference is invalid.

Key Terms
إرادة أزلية
مريد الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) God would be described with both goodness and evilness.
B) God would be all-powerful.
C) Evil would not exist.
D) Human free will would be negated.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection rests on a claimed rational principle that the willer's moral quality matches the willed act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows the Mu'tazila arguing that a willer's moral character is tied to what they will. When you look at your own will—the desires you nurture and the actions you choose—what do they say about your character before Allah? Are there any small sins you casually will, thinking they don't define you? What will you do today to align your will with what Allah wills?
?
The Mu'tazila concluded that God's perfect nature prevents Him from willing evil. If you truly believed that every trial or evil you face is not willed by Allah for its own sake but rather permitted with wisdom, how would that change your trust in His decree during hardship? Write down one specific trial you are facing and commit to trusting that Allah's will for you is ultimately good.
?
The verse quoted (Ghafir 31) says Allah does not will injustice for His servants. Reflect: Have you ever accused Allah of being unjust in your heart when something bad happened to you? Based on this lesson, what is the truth about Allah's will? Repent from any doubt and affirm your belief that Allah only wills what is just.

The Ash'ariyya Rebuttal: The Premise is Not Universal
Ash'ariyya rebut the Mu'tazila premise as non-universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ariyya criticize the Mu'tazila premise by showing it is culturally conditioned and not applicable to God.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila premise is a mere custom, not a necessary truth.

The Ash'ariyya respond by first questioning the universal validity of the Mu'tazila premise. They argue it is not self-evident but a culturally accepted generalization. They provide counterexamples from everyday language and then introduce a key distinction between necessary and acquired will to show why the analogy fails for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ariyya rebuttal
Read the full Arabic passage of the Ash'ariyya response. Notice how they challenge the nature of the premise.
2
Identify the counterexamples
The Ash'ariyya list several cases where the premise does not hold: willing ignorance does not make one ignorant, willing motion does not make one moving, etc. Note these.
3
Understand the distinction
They then explain that temporal will can be necessary or acquired. Only acquired will carries moral responsibility. This distinction undermines the analogy to God's eternal will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'ariyya distinguish between necessary will (like a king's natural inclination) and acquired will (our choices). Which type dominates your own relationship with obedience to God? Are you obeying because it has become a habit or because you truly choose it each time? What would it take to transform your necessary will into an acquired one, and why does it matter for your accountability before God?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that because willing worship makes one a worshipper in the human case, the same must apply to God. Have you ever made an assumption about God based solely on your own experience without considering that God is fundamentally different? What might be the consequences of clinging to such an assumption, and how can you guard against it?
?
If the Mu'tazila premise were true, then God willing goodness would make Him 'good' in the same way we are good. But the Ash'ariyya reject this. Do you secretly believe that God owes you a certain kind of treatment because you think He should act according to human standards? How does this passage challenge you to rethink your expectations of God?

God's Will Attaches to Existence, Not Moral Qualities
Ash'ari defines God's will as relating to existence, not moral qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's eternal will attaches to actions only insofar as they are existent, not as morally good or evil, and that existence as such is good.

Core Idea
God wills existence, and existence is good; evil is a lack.

This passage from the Ash'ari scholar explains a key distinction: God's will does not directly will morally qualified actions; it wills them as existent. Since existence is good, God wills only good. This teaches us that evil is not a positive entity but a privation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author contrasts 'وجود' (existence) with 'عدم' (non-existence).
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between God's will attaching to actions as 'مكلف به' (obligations) and as 'متجدد متخصص بالوجود' (renewed, specified by existence). Find these phrases and note their difference.
3
Understand the concept of evil as privation
The author states that from the aspect of existence, evil is not pure evil; 'لا يتحقق في الوجود شر محض' (no pure evil is realized). Reflect on how evil is defined here as lack, not a positive thing.
This is a key Ash'ari principle.
4
Connect to the broader theology
Ask yourself: how does this explanation protect God's goodness while accounting for evil in the world? The commentary will help you see the theological stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسر في ذلك : أن الإرادة الأزلية لم تتعلق بالمراد من أفعال العباد من حيث هو مكلف به ؛ إما طاعة وإما معصية، وإما خيرًا، وإما شرا، بل لا يتعلق به من حيث هو فعل العبد وكسبه على الوجه الذي ينسب إليه ، فإن إرادة فعل الغير من فعله تمن وشهوة وإنما يتعلق به من حيث هو متجدد متخصص بالوجود حيث هو دون العدم، متقدر بقدر دون قدر، وهو من هذا الوجه غير موصوف بالخير والشر، وإن أطلق لفظ الخير على الوجود من حيث هو وجود فذلك إطلاق بمعنى يخالف ما تنازعنا فيه فالباري تعالى مريد الوجود من حيث هو وجود، والوجود من حيث هو وجود خير فهو مريد الخير وبيده الخير. وأما الوجه الذي ينسب إلى العبد هو صفة لفعله بالنسبة إلى قدرته واستطاعته وزمانه ومكانه وتكليفه وهو من هذا الوجه غير مراد للباري تعالى وغير مقدور له ، ولما تقرر عندنا بالبراهين السابقة أنه تعالى خالق أعمال العباد كما هو خالق الكون كله، وإنما هو خالق بالاختيار والإرادة لا بالطبع والذات، فكان مريداً مختارًا لتجدد الوجود وحدوث الموجود، ثم الوجود خير كله من حيث هو وجود، فكان مريد الخير، وأما الشر فمن حيث هو موجود فقد شارك الخير فهو من ذلك الوجه خير ومراد، وعلى هذا لا يتحقق في الوجود شر محض، فهو تعالى مريد الوجود ومريد الخير والعبد يريد الخير والشر، وعن هذا قال الحكماء : الشر داخل في القضاء والإرادة بالعرض لا بالذات وبالقصد الثاني لا بالقصد الأول، فإن الشر عندهم إما عدم وجود، أو عدم كمال الوجود ، وإنما الداخل في القضاء والإرادة بالقصد الأول هو الوجود، وكمال الوجود ثم قد يكون الوجود على كمال أول ومتوجه إلى كمال ثان، وقد يكون على كمال مطلق كالعقول المفارقة التي هي تامة كاملة بأعيانها في الخير المحض الذي لا شر فيه، وما هو على كمال أول؛ أي هو بالقوة
And the secret in that is that the eternal will did not attach to the willed from the actions of the servants in terms of it being a matter of obligation, whether obedience or disobedience, good or evil. Rather, it attaches to it only in terms of it being the act of the servant and his acquisition (kasb) in the manner that is attributed to him. For willing the act of another from his action is aversion and appetite; rather, it attaches to it in terms of it being something new, specifically characterized by existence where it is rather than non-existence, measured by a measure rather than another measure. And from this aspect, it is not described with good and evil. Even if the term 'good' is applied to existence qua existence, that is an application in a sense different from what we are disputing about. So the Exalted God is the willer of existence qua existence, and existence qua existence is good, so He is the willer of good, and in His hand is good. As for the aspect that is attributed to the servant, it is a description of his action with respect to his power and capacity, his time, place, and obligation. And from this aspect, it is not willed by the Exalted God and not within His power. And since it has been established with us by previous proofs that He, exalted, is the creator of the deeds of the servants just as He is the creator of all existence, and He creates by choice and will, not by nature and essence, so He is a willer, a chooser for the renewal of existence and the coming-into-being of the existent. Then existence is all good qua existence, so He is the willer of good. As for evil, since it insofar as it is existent, it has shared with good, so from that aspect it is good and willed. On this basis, no pure evil is realized in existence. So He, exalted, is the willer of existence and the willer of good, while the servant wills good and evil. And about this the philosophers said: Evil enters into decree and will accidentally, not essentially, and by secondary intention, not by primary intention. For evil, according to them, is either non-existence or absence of perfection of existence. That which enters into decree and will by primary intention is only existence and the perfection of existence. Then existence may be upon a first perfection and directed towards a second perfection, and may be upon absolute perfection, like the separate intellects which are complete and perfect in their essences in pure good with no evil in them. And that which is upon a first perfection, i.e., it is in potentiality...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage provides a deeper theological explanation of how God's eternal will relates to actions without implying a direct willing of evil. The author clarifies that God's will does not attach to actions insofar as they are morally qualified (obedience/disobedience) for the servant; rather, it attaches to them insofar as they are existent, new, and measured. Existence as such is good, so God wills existence and thus good. Evil, being a lack of existence or perfection, is not willed essentially but only accidentally. The philosophers' view is cited: evil enters divine decree by secondary intention, not primary. The passage also reaffirms God's creative role by choice. It concludes by noting that no pure evil exists in reality, and that the servant's will (which can choose evil) is distinct from God's will, which always seeks good.

Key Terms
إرادة أزلية
خير
شر
عدم وجود
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God directly wills evil actions like disobedience.
Correction: God wills actions only insofar as they are existent, not as morally evil. Evil is a privation of good, not a positive entity directly willed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, from which aspect does God's will attach to human actions?
A) As morally good or evil
B) As existent, new, and measured
C) As obligations of the servant
D) As acts of obedience only

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will is directed at existence, which is good; evil, being privation, is not directly willed but only accidentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself is good because God wills it, how does this change the way you view your own existence, especially in moments of suffering or hardship? Will you thank Him today simply for being alive?
?
Have you ever blamed God for an evil event, as if He directly willed it? How does this passage challenge that assumption and call you to repent of such thoughts?
?
The passage says no pure evil is realized in existence. Can you identify a trial in your life where, despite the pain, you can see something good in its existence? How does this truth affect your trust in God's will?

Evil Enters Divine Decree Accidentally: The Philosophers' View
Philosophers' view on evil entering decree accidentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation that evil is included in divine decree only accidentally and by secondary intention, as privation of existence or perfection.

Core Idea
Evil is not willed by God directly; it is a byproduct of a world of goods.

The author now cites the philosophers (likely Ibn Sina and Al-Farabi) to explain that evil enters the divine decree only accidentally. Evil is defined as non-existence or absence of perfection. This aligns with the Ash'ari position that God's will is entirely good and that evil is not a positive entity but a lack.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read the Arabic text from 'وعن هذا قال الحكماء' to the end. Note the terms 'بالعرض' (accidentally) and 'بالذات' (essentially).
The philosophers here are Islamic Peripatetics like Ibn Sina.
2
Understand the two types of evil
The philosophers define evil as either 'عدم وجود' (non-existence) or 'عدم كمال الوجود' (absence of perfection). Identify both and consider examples.
3
Grasp the distinction between primary and secondary intention
Evil enters by 'القصد الثاني' (secondary intention) not by 'القصد الأول' (primary intention). Think about what God primarily wills: existence and perfection.
This distinction preserves God's goodness.
4
Reflect on the example of separate intellects
The philosophers mention 'العقول المفارقة' (separate intellects) as pure good with no evil. This shows that some beings are entirely good, while material beings have potential for evil due to lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a personal tragedy or witness injustice, do you instinctively blame God? How does the philosophers' teaching that evil is an accidental byproduct of a good creation challenge you to trust His wisdom and goodness even in suffering? Will you now consciously remind yourself that God primarily wills good, and evil is not His direct intention?
?
If evil is merely the absence of good, how does that reframe your view of your own sins? Do you see them as positive rebellions or as failures to attain the good? How does this perspective affect the sincerity of your repentance—will you now strive to fill your life with that which is truly good (existence and perfection) rather than merely avoiding evil?
?
The philosophers say separate intellects are pure good with no evil. How does your own soul compare? Are you content with merely avoiding major sins, or are you actively seeking absolute perfection in your moral and spiritual life? What is one step you can take today to move from 'first perfection' toward 'second perfection'?

Evil as Subordinate to Divine Will
Shahrastani explains evil as subordinate to divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how evil exists in the world only as a subordinate and accidental consequence of God's primary will for existence and good.

Core Idea
Evil is not directly willed by God; it is the unavoidable shadow of His pursuit of existence.

This passage tackles one of the most challenging questions in theology: how can evil exist if God is all-good and all-powerful? Al-Shahrastani provides a sophisticated answer by distinguishing between God's primary intention (existence and good) and the secondary, accidental effects that include evil. This lesson unpacks his argument, grounded in the concept of the eternal will and the nature of existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core distinction
First, note how the eternal will attaches to actions not as morally qualified but as temporally originated existents. This sets up the key distinction between existence (good) and the moral qualities attached by humans.
2
Identify the two aspects of acts
Distinguish between the aspect of existence (willed by God) and the aspect of human acquisition (not directly willed). This is crucial for the argument.
3
Trace the philosophers' formulation
Find where Shahrastani cites the philosophers: evil enters the decree accidentally and by second intention. Understand what 'first intention' and 'second intention' mean in this context.
4
Analyze the rain example
Work through the example of rain: the universal good (order) is intended by first intention; the destruction of a house is an accidental evil by second intention. This concretizes the abstract argument.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this doctrine preserves divine justice and wisdom: a universal good with a particular evil is wiser than preventing evil by preventing all existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسر في ذلك: أن الإرادة الأزلية لم تتعلق بالمراد من أفعال العباد من حيث هو مكلف به؛ إما طاعة وإما معصية، وإما خيرًا، وإما شرا، بل لا يتعلق به من حيث هو فعل العبد وكسبه على الوجه الذي ينسب إليه، فإن إرادة فعل الغير من فعله تمن وشهوة وإنما يتعلق به من حيث هو متجدد متخصص بالوجود حيث هو دون العدم، متقدر بقدر دون قدر، وهو من هذا الوجه غير موصوف بالخير والشر، وإن أطلق لفظ الخير على الوجود من حيث هو وجود فذلك إطلاق بمعنى يخالف ما تنازعنا فيه فالباري تعالى مريد الوجود من حيث هو وجود، والوجود من حيث هو وجود خير فهو مريد الخير وبيده الخير. وأما الوجه الذي ينسب إلى العبد هو صفة لفعله بالنسبة إلى قدرته واستطاعته وزمانه ومكانه وتكليفه وهو من هذا الوجه غير مراد للباري تعالى وغير مقدور له، ولما تقرر عندنا بالبراهين السابقة أنه تعالى خالق أعمال العباد كما هو خالق الكون كله، وإنما هو خالق بالاختيار والإرادة لا بالطبع والذات، فكان مريداً مختارًا لتجدد الوجود وحدوث الموجود، ثم الوجود خير كله من حيث هو وجود، فكان مريد الخير، وأما الشر فمن حيث هو موجود فقد شارك الخير فهو من ذلك الوجه خير ومراد، وعلى هذا لا يتحقق في الوجود شر محض، فهو تعالى مريد الوجود ومريد الخير والعبد يريد الخير والشر، وعن هذا قال الحكماء: الشر داخل في القضاء والإرادة بالعرض لا بالذات وبالقصد الثاني لا بالقصد الأول، فإن الشر عندهم إما عدم وجود، أو عدم كمال الوجود، وإنما الداخل في القضاء والإرادة بالقصد الأول هو الوجود، وكمال الوجود ثم قد يكون الوجود على كمال أول ومتوجه إلى كمال ثان، وقد يكون على كمال مطلق كالعقول المفارقة التي هي تامة كاملة بأعيانها في الخير المحض الذي لا شر فيه، وما هو على كمال أول؛ أي هو بالقوة على كمال إلى أن يصير بالفعل على كمال ثان فيقع من مصادمات أحوال السلوك المبدأ إلى الكمال أحوال هي شرور ومضار، كما تقع أحوال هي سرور ومنافع، والإرادة الأزلية والعناية الربانية تتعلق بالأمرين والقسمين جميعا، ولكن أحد المتعلقين على سبيل التضمن والاستتباع والعرضية ويسمى ذلك مرادا ومقصودا بالقصد الثاني والمتعلق الثاني على سبيل الوضع والأصالة والذاتية فيسمى ذلك مرادا ومقصودا بالقصد الأول. هذا، كما يعلم أن المقصود الكلي من إنزال المطر من السماء نظام العالم وانتظام الوجود، وذلك هو الخير مطلقا، ثم إن خرب بذلك بيت عجوز قد أشرف على الانهدام، أو ماتت العجوز وقد بلغت إلى شرف الموت كان ذلك شراً بالإضافة لا بالأصالة، وبالقصد الثاني لا بالقصد الأول، ووجود خير كلي مع شر جزئي أقرب إلى الحكمة من لا وجود له لا يقع الشر الجزئي، فإن عدمه مما يورث الفساد في نظام الموجودات، فهو أكثر شرًا وأشد ضرٍّ.
And the secret of that is: that the eternal will did not attach to what is willed of the actions of servants insofar as it is something legally obligated—whether obedience or disobedience, whether good or evil—rather, it does not attach to it insofar as it is the servant's act and acquisition in the manner that is attributed to him. For willing another's act from his act is a favor and desire, and rather it attaches to it insofar as it is temporally originated, specified with existence where it is rather than non-existence, measured by a measure rather than a measure, and from this aspect it is not described as good or evil. And if the term 'good' is applied to existence insofar as it is existence, that is an application in a sense that differs from what we are disputing. So the Most High Creator is willing existence insofar as it is existence, and existence insofar as it is existence is good, so He is willing good, and in His hand is good. As for the aspect attributed to the servant, it is a description of his act in relation to his power and capacity, his time, his place, and his obligation, and from this aspect it is not willed by the Most High Creator nor within His power. And since it has been established for us by preceding proofs that He, Most High, is the Creator of servants' deeds just as He is the Creator of the whole universe, and He is Creator by choice and will, not by nature and essence, so He is willing and choosing for the renewal of existence and the occurrence of the existent. Then existence is all good insofar as it is existence, so He is willing good. As for evil, insofar as it is existent, it has shared in good, so from that aspect it is good and willed. And on this basis, no pure evil is realized in existence. So He, Most High, is willing existence and willing good, while the servant wills good and evil. And concerning this, the philosophers said: Evil enters into the decree and will accidentally, not essentially, and by second intention, not by first intention. For evil according to them is either non-existence of existence or non-existence of the perfection of existence, and what enters into the decree and will by first intention is existence and the perfection of existence. Then existence may be in a first perfection and directed toward a second perfection, and it may be in absolute perfection like the separate intellects which are complete and perfect in themselves in pure good in which there is no evil. And what is in a first perfection—i.e., it is in potentiality for perfection—until it becomes actual in a second perfection, then from the collisions of the conditions of the path from the beginning to perfection, conditions occur that are evils and harms, just as conditions occur that are joys and benefits. And the eternal will and divine providence attach to both matters and both divisions collectively, but one of the attached things is in the mode of inclusion and following and accidentally, and that is called willed and intended by second intention; and the second attached thing is in the mode of position and originality and essentiality, and that is called willed and intended by first intention. This is as it is known that the overall purpose of sending down rain from the sky is the order of the world and the regulation of existence, and that is good absolutely. Then if through that a house of an old woman on the verge of collapse is destroyed, or the old woman who has reached the brink of death dies, that is an evil by addition, not by originality, and by second intention, not by first intention. And the existence of a universal good with a particular evil is closer to wisdom than that there be no existence at all such that the particular evil does not occur. For its non-existence would cause corruption in the system of existing things, so it is more evil and more harmful.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical-theological argument by al-Shahrastani explaining how divine will relates to evil. The author begins by stating the core secret: the eternal will (al-irāda al-azaliyya) does not attach to human acts in their moral quality as obedience or disobedience, good or evil. Instead, it attaches to them insofar as they are temporally originated existents, measured and specified. From this aspect, the act is not described as good or evil. If we call existence 'good' in general, that is a different usage. Thus God wills existence as such, and existence is good, so He wills good.

Then he distinguishes the aspect attributed to the servant: the act as it relates to the servant's power, time, place, and obligation—from this aspect it is not willed by God nor within His power (i.e., not directly created). However, based on earlier proofs, God creates all deeds by choice and will. So He wills the renewal of existence, which is good, and evil insofar as it exists shares in good and is therefore from that aspect good and willed. Hence no pure evil exists.

He then cites the philosophers' formulation: 'Evil enters into the decree and will accidentally, not essentially, and by second intention, not first intention.' Evil is either non-existence of existence or non-existence of perfection. What enters by first intention is existence and perfection. He explains that existence can be at various stages of perfection. The separate intellects are in absolute perfection, pure good. Other things are in potentiality for perfection, and in the process of actualization, collisions occur causing evils and harms, along with joys and benefits. The eternal will and divine providence attach to both aspects, but one as secondary (accidental) and the other as primary (essential).

He illustrates with an example: the overall purpose of rain is the order of the world, which is absolute good. If it destroys an old woman's house or kills her, that is an evil by addition (relative), not original, and by second intention. The existence of universal good with particular evil is wiser than total non-existence that would cause more corruption. So the particular evil is tolerated for the greater good.

Key Terms
الإرادة الأزلية (eternal will)
القضاء (decree)
الإرادة بالعرض (accidental will)
القصد الأول (first intention)
القصد الثاني (second intention)
الخير والشر (good and evil)
الوجود (existence)
العدم (non-existence)
العناية الربانية (divine providence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God directly wills evil acts.
Correction: The passage explains that evil is willed only accidentally and by second intention as a consequence of willing existence and its perfections.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does evil enter into the divine decree and will?
A) Essentially and by first intention.
B) Accidentally and by second intention.
C) As pure evil directly willed by God.
D) Evil does not enter the decree at all.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not directly willed by God; it enters the divine decree accidentally as a necessary consequence of willing existence and its perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering, do you instinctively blame God or question His justice? This lesson teaches that evil is not His primary intention but an accidental byproduct of a greater good. Can you commit today to repeating this truth in your heart during your next trial, and to asking God to help you see the hidden good?
?
Shahrastani gives the example of rain destroying an old woman's house—a particular evil caused by a universal good. Think of a personal loss that felt purely negative. Can you prayerfully consider what greater purpose it might have served? Will you write down one possible good that came from that loss as an act of faith?
?
The passage states that 'no pure evil is realized in existence.' Are there hurts you hold that you have labeled as absolute evil, with no redeeming value? What would change if you believed this statement fully? Will you ask God to soften your heart to see His wisdom even in your deepest pain?

Primary and Secondary Objects of Divine Will
The author explains how divine will includes good and evil, evil being secondary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine will encompasses both good and evil, with evil being a secondary, accidental object of will.

Core Idea
Evil is not willed for itself but as a necessary consequence of a greater good.

This passage explains a crucial nuance in Islamic theology: how can a good God will evil? The author clarifies that while both good and evil are within God's will, they are not on the same level. One is intended essentially, the other merely consequentially. The rain example brings the point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'first intention' and 'second intention'.
2
Identify the two categories of divine will
The passage distinguishes between what is willed by first intention (essential, original) and by second intention (accidental, consequential). Find how the author contrasts them using terms like 'by way of inclusion' and 'by way of placement'.
3
Reflect on the rain example
The example of rain shows how a universal good (order of the world) may cause partial evil (destruction of a house). Ask yourself: Why does the author say the partial evil is willed by second intention? How does this illustrate the overall principle?
This connects to the idea that existence of good with some evil is better than no good at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على كمال إلى أن يصير بالفعل على كمال ثان فيقع من مصادمات أحوال السلوك المبدأ إلى الكمال أحوال هي شرور ومضار، كما تقع أحوال هي سرور ومنافع، والإرادة الأزلية والعناية الربانية تتعلق بالأمرين والقسمين جميعا، ولكن أحد المتعلقين على سبيل التضمن والاستتباع، والعرضية ويسمى ذلك مرادا ومقصودا بالقصد الثاني والمتعلق الثاني على سبيل الوضع والأصالة والذاتية فيسمى ذلك مرادا ومقصودا بالقصد الأول. هذا، كما يعلم أن المقصود الكلي من إنزال المطر من السماء نظام العالم وانتظام الوجود، وذلك هو الخير مطلقا، ثم إن خرب بذلك بيت عجوز قد أشرف على الانهدام، أو ماتت العجوز وقد بلغت إلى شرف الموت كان ذلك شراً بالإضافة لا بالأصالة، وبالقصد الثاني لا بالقصد الأول، ووجود خير كلي مع شر جزئي أقرب إلى الحكمة من لا وجود له لا يقع الشر الجزئي، فإن عدمه مما يورث الفساد في نظام الموجودات، فهو أكثر شرًا وأشد ضرٍّ.
... to a perfection, until it becomes actual in a second perfection. Then from collisions of the states of the journey from the beginning to perfection, there occur states that are evils and harms, just as there occur states that are pleasures and benefits. The eternal will and divine care pertain to both matters and both divisions altogether, but one of the two objects is by way of inclusion and consequence and accidentality, and that is called 'willed' and 'intended' by the second intention; and the second object is by way of placement and originality and essentiality, and that is called 'willed' and 'intended' by the first intention. This, as is known: the universal purpose of sending down rain from heaven is the order of the world and the regularity of existence, and that is the absolute good. Then if thereby a house of an old woman on the verge of collapse is destroyed, or if the old woman who has reached the brink of death dies, that is evil accidentally, not essentially, and by the second intention, not by the first intention. The existence of a universal good with a partial evil is closer to wisdom than the non-existence of it such that the partial evil does not occur, for the absence of it (the rain) would cause corruption in the system of existents, so it is more evil and more harmful.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage explains how divine will relates to good and evil, distinguishing between primary and secondary intentions. The author argues that as things move toward perfection, collisions produce both good and evil states. The eternal will and divine care encompass both, but one is included accidentally and consequentially (the second intention), while the other is intended essentially and originally (the first intention). The example of rain illustrates this: rain's purpose is the world's order and good. If it accidentally destroys a house or kills an old woman, that evil is accidental, not essential. It is better to have the universal good with partial evil than to prevent the rain entirely, which would cause greater harm. Thus, evil is not willed for itself but is a necessary byproduct of a greater good.

الإرادة الأزلية (al-irāda al-azaliyya)
العناية الربانية (al-‘ināya al-rabbāniyya)
القصد الأول (al-qaṣd al-awwal)
القصد الثاني (al-qaṣd al-thānī)
الخير الكلي (al-khayr al-kullī)
الشر الجزئي (al-sharr al-juz’ī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God does not will evil at all, or that He wills it directly and essentially.
Correction: God wills evil indirectly and accidentally, as a necessary consequence of a greater good. The passage shows that evil is willed by the second intention, not the first.

Checkpoint

A) It is willed essentially and by the first intention.
B) It is willed accidentally and by the second intention.
C) It is not willed at all; it merely occurs.
D) It is willed as a punishment for the old woman.

Key Takeaway
Evil is not willed for its own sake but as an unavoidable byproduct of a greater good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a personal tragedy strikes, do you instinctively resent God, or can you honestly see it as a second intention—willed not for its own sake but for a greater good you may not yet see? What would it take for you to trust that the partial evil you suffer is outweighed by a universal good you cannot perceive?
?
Think of a recent minor annoyance or hardship you complained about. Does this lesson convict you that your complaint might be rooted in ignoring God's first intention—the vast good He is bringing about? Will you commit to actively reframing such moments as part of His wise plan, rather than focusing on the secondary evil?
?
If you truly believed that God never wills evil for its own sake, how would your reaction to the suffering of others change? Would you be more patient, more merciful, more trusting? What one step can you take this week to live out this conviction in the face of injustice or pain you witness?

The Mu'tazila Argument on Command and Will and the Ash'ari Response
Mu'tazila command implies will; Ash'ari rebuttal based on knowledge of occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that divine command implies will and the Ash'ari rebuttal distinguishing command from will based on knowledge of occurrence.

Core Idea
God may command what He does not will, because will only applies to what actually occurs.

This lesson presents a classic kalam debate between the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya about the relationship between God's command and His will. The Mu'tazila argue that commanding necessarily implies willing, while the Ash'ariyya respond that command does not require will if the commanded act is known not to occur. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on divine will and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first part carefully. The Mu'tazila present several logical steps to prove that every commander wills what he commands.
Note the example of Abu Jahl.
2
Read the Ash'ari response
Then read the Ash'ari rebuttal where they reject the premise and introduce the condition of knowledge of occurrence.
3
Compare the two positions
Compare how each side defines will (irāda) and its relation to command and knowledge.
Focus on the concept of takhṣīṣ (specification).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : كل آمر بالشيء فهو مريد له والرب تعالى أمر عباده بالطاعة فهو مريد لها ؛ إذ من المستحيل أن يأمر عبده بالطاعة ثم لا يريدها، والجمع بين اقتضاء الطاعة وطلبها بالأمر بها، وبين كراهية وقوعها جمع بين نقيضين، وذلك بمثابة الأمر بالشيء والنهي عنه في حالة واحدة ؛ إذ لا فرق بين قول القائل : أمرك بكذا وأكره منك فعله، وبين قوله : آمرك بكذا وأنهاك عنه، وإذا وقع الاتفاق بأن الله تعالى آمر عباده بالطاعة وجب أن يكون مريدا لها كارها لضدها من المعصية، وإذا كان الآمر بالشيء مريدا له كان الناهي عن الشيء كارها له. والذي يحقق ذلك أن الآمر بالشيء يقتضي من المأمور حصول المأمور به، والإرادة تقتضي تخصيص المأمور به بالوجود ومن المحال اقتضاء الحصول لشيء واقتضاء ضد ذلك منه، فلو قلنا : إن الباري تعالى أمر أبا جهل بالإيمان وأراد منه الكفر أدى ذلك إلى اقتضاء الإيمان منه بحكم الأمر، واقتضاء الكفر منه بحكم الإرادة محال، ويخرج على هذه القاعدة استرواحكم إلى العلم، فإنه يجوز أن يأمر بخلاف المعلوم؛ لأن العلم ليس فيه اقتضاء وطلب، وإنما هو يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به بخلاف الإرادة فإنها مقتضية فيرد الأمر على خلاف العلم ولا يرد على خلاف الإرادة. قالت الأشعرية : لسنا نسلم أن كل آمر بالشيء مريد حصوله، بل كل آمر بالشيء عالم بحصوله مريد له حصولا، وكل آمر ليعلم حصول ضده لا يكون مريدا لحصوله، فإن الإرادة على خلاف العلم تعطيل لحكم الإرادة وتغيير لأخص وصفها، وقد بينا أن أخص وصفها التخصيص، وحكمها إنما يتعلق بالمتجدد من المقدورات والمتخصص من المقدورات، فإذا علم الأمر أن المأمور به لا يحصل قط ولا يتجدد ولا يتخصص قط فيستحيل أن يريده فإنها توجد ولا متعلق لها يتعلق ولا أثر لتعلقها وذلك محال، ولو كانت الإرادة خاصيتها أن تتعلق بالممكن فقط كالقدرة لكان جائزاً أن تتعلق بخلاف المعلوم. ومن العجب : أن متعلق القدرة أعم من متعلق الإرادة، فإن الجائز الممكن من حيث هو ممكن متعلق القدرة والمتجدد من جملة الممكنات هو متعلق الإرادة، والمتجدد أخص من الممكن.
The Mu'tazila said: Everyone who commands something wills it, and the Lord, exalted, commanded His servants to obey, so He wills it; for it is impossible that He command His servant to obey and then not will it. Combining the requiring of obedience and requesting it by commanding it, along with hating its occurrence, is a combination of two opposites, and that is like commanding something and forbidding it in one state; for there is no difference between one who says 'I command you to do such-and-such and I hate your doing it' and one who says 'I command you to do such-and-such and I forbid you from it.' And if it is agreed that God commands His servants to obey, then He must be willing it and hating its opposite, which is disobedience. And if the commander of something wills it, then the forbidder of something hates it. What verifies that is that the commander of something demands from the commanded the occurrence of the commanded thing, and will requires the specification of the commanded thing to existence. It is impossible to require the occurrence of something and to require the opposite of that from him. So if we say that the Exalted Creator commanded Abu Jahl to believe and willed from him disbelief, that would entail requiring belief from him by the ruling of the command and requiring disbelief from him by the ruling of will, which is impossible. And based on this rule, you may relax regarding knowledge, for it is permissible that He command contrary to what is known, because knowledge does not involve requiring and requesting; rather it relates to the known as it is, unlike will, for it is requiring, so the command can oppose knowledge but cannot oppose will. The Ash'ariyya said: We do not grant that every commander of something wills its occurrence. Rather, every commander of something who knows its occurrence wills its occurrence, and every commander who knows the occurrence of its opposite is not willing its occurrence. For will contrary to knowledge would nullify the ruling of will and change its most specific attribute. We have already explained that its most specific attribute is specification, and its ruling pertains only to what is renewed among the things that are determined and what is specified among the things that are determined. So if the commander knows that the commanded thing never occurs, nor is renewed, nor is specified at all, then it is impossible that He will it, for then it would exist and have no object to which it relates and no effect of its relation, and that is impossible. And if will's property were to relate only to the possible, like power, then it would be permissible for it to relate contrary to the known. And astonishingly, the object of power is more general than the object of will: for the permissible possible, insofar as it is possible, is the object of power, while what is renewed among the possibles is the object of will; and the renewed is more specific than the possible.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and a rebuttal from the Ash'ariyya regarding the relationship between divine command and will. The Mu'tazila argue that every commander wills what he commands, because commanding implies a demand for the commanded act, and will is the principle that specifies it to existence. They claim it's impossible for God to command obedience and not will it, as that would combine opposites. They illustrate that commanding and hating is like commanding and forbidding. Therefore, since God commands obedience, He must will it and hate disobedience. They further argue that if God commanded Abu Jahl to believe but willed his disbelief, that would be contradictory. They note that command can oppose knowledge but not will, because knowledge is passive while will is active. The Ash'ariyya respond by rejecting the premise: not every commander wills the occurrence of what he commands. Instead, a commander wills it only if he knows it will occur. Will's specific attribute is specification (takhṣīṣ), which applies only to things that actually come into existence. If something is known never to occur, willing it would be impossible because there would be no object for the will. They compare will to power: power relates to all possible things, but will is more specific, relating only to those possibles that actually come into being. Thus, God may command something He knows will not happen, but He does not will it. This preserves both the command (which has a purpose even if not fulfilled) and the will (which is tied to actual events).

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الأشعرية
الإرادة
الأمر
الكراهة
التخصيص
القدرة
الممكن
المتجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe God's commands are meaningless if He does not will them.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that commands have purposes such as establishing proof (iḥtijāj) and conveying obligations, even if the commanded act does not occur.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why can God command something He does not will?
A) Because will and command are identical.
B) Because power is more general than will, so God can command what He does not will.
C) Because will relates only to what actually occurs and is known to occur, while command may have other purposes.
D) Because God can will contradictory things.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ariyya distinguish command (amr) from will (irāda) by tying will to actual occurrence, so God can command what He knows will not happen but does not will it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often command yourself to do good deeds and then fail. In light of this lesson, is it true that you willed those deeds? Or did you merely command without will? Examine your heart: when you say 'I will do this' but never do, what does that reveal about your sincerity before the One whose will is perfectly aligned with what He knows will occur?
?
If God commands what He does not will, and you are created in His image (in terms of attributes like will and command), where in your life do you command what you do not actually will? How can you align your commands with your will to be more truthful?
?
The Ash'ari say that will's most specific attribute is specification to what actually occurs. Look at your own will over the past week: how many things did you 'will' that never happened? Are you comfortable with the gap between your will and reality? What does that teach you about the perfection of God's will—that nothing occurs outside His will?
