Divine Command and Will: First Objection and Response
The author argues command implies will, then presents and answers an Ash'ari objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational relationship between divine command and divine will, and how the Ash'ari objection qualifies that relationship without denying it.

Core Idea
Command implies will, but only if the commander knows the commanded will actually occur.

This passage presents a key theological debate: does God's command to a person imply that God wills that person to obey? The author argues for a necessary connection, using the example of Abu Jahl. But the Ash'ari school objects, pointing to cases where God commands something He knows will never happen. Their response refines the understanding of divine will, showing it attaches only to what actually comes to be.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the text's structure reveal itself before analysis.
Pay attention to the flow from proof to objection to response.
2
Identify the author's proof
Locate the author's initial argument that command implies will. Note the reductio ad absurdum with Abu Jahl.
What makes this argument ‘impossible’ according to the author?
3
Understand the Ash'ari objection
Read the Ash'ari objection carefully. They do not deny the link entirely; they condition it on knowledge of occurrence. Look for key terms like ‘specification’ (takhsis) and ‘coming to be’ (mutajaddid).
Why do they say will cannot attach to something that never occurs?
4
See the author's response
The author responds by comparing will to power. Notice how the scope of will is more restricted—it attaches only to what actually comes to be, whereas power attaches to all possibles.
Does this response affirm or undermine the original proof?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق ذلك أن الآمر بالشيء يقتضي من المأمور حصول المأمور به، والإرادة تقتضي تخصيص المأمور به بالوجود ومن المحال اقتضاء الحصول لشيء واقتضاء ضد ذلك منه، فلو قلنا إن الباري تعالى أمر أبا جهل بالإيمان وأراد منه الكفر أدى ذلك إلى اقتضاء الإيمان منه بحكم الأمر، واقتضاء الكفر منه بحكم الإرادة محال، ويخرج على هذه القاعدة استرواحكم إلى العلم، فإنه يجوز أن يأمر بخلاف المعلوم؛ لأن العلم ليس فيه اقتضاء وطلب، وإنما هو يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به بخلاف الإرادة فإنها مقتضية فيرد الأمر على خلاف العلم ولا يرد على خلاف الإرادة. قالت الأشعرية: لسنا نسلم أن كل آمر بالشيء مريد حصوله، بل كل آمر بالشيء عالم بحصوله مريد له حصولا، وكل آمر يعلم حصول ضده لا يكون مريدا لحصوله، فإن الإرادة على خلاف العلم تعطيل لحكم الإرادة وتغيير لأخص وصفها، وقد بينا أن أخص وصفها التخصيص، وحكمها إنما يتعلق بالمتجدد من المقدورات والمتخصص من المقدورات، فإذا علم الأمر أن المأمور به لا يحصل قط ولا يتجدد ولا يتخصص قط فيستحيل أن يريده فإنها توجد ولا متعلق لها يتعلق ولا أثر لتعلقها وذلك محال، ولو كانت الإرادة خاصيتها أن تتعلق بالممكن فقط كالقدرة لكان جائزا أن تتعلق بخلاف المعلوم. ومن العجب أن متعلق القدرة أعم من متعلق الإرادة، فإن الجائز الممكن من حيث هو ممكن متعلق القدرة والمتجدد من جملة الممكنات هو متعلق الإرادة، والمتجدد أخص من الممكن.
And what verifies that is that the commander of something requires from the commanded the occurrence of the commanded thing, and the will requires the specification of the commanded thing to existence, and it is impossible to require the occurrence of something and require its opposite from it. So if we say that the Exalted Creator commanded Abu Jahl to believe and willed from him disbelief, that would lead to requiring belief from him by virtue of command and requiring disbelief from him by virtue of will, which is impossible. And on this principle your resort to knowledge is excepted, for it is permissible that He command contrary to what is known, because knowledge does not involve requirement and demand; rather it pertains to the known as it is, unlike will, for it is demanding, so the command may be contrary to knowledge but not contrary to will. The Ash'ariyya said: We do not concede that every commander of something wills its occurrence; rather every commander of something knows its occurrence and wills its occurrence, and every commander who knows the occurrence of its opposite does not will its occurrence, for willing contrary to knowledge is nullification of the ruling of will and alteration of its most specific attribute. And we have shown that its most specific attribute is specification, and its ruling pertains only to that which comes to be among the possibles and that which is specified among the possibles. So if the commander knows that the commanded thing does not occur at all, nor comes to be nor becomes specified at all, then it is impossible that He wills it, for then it would exist with no object to which it pertains and no effect of its pertaining, and that is impossible. And if the will had the property of pertaining only to the possible, like power, it would be permissible for it to pertain contrary to what is known. And it is remarkable that the object of power is more general than the object of will: for the possible insofar as it is possible is the object of power, and that which comes to be among the possibles is the object of will, and that which comes to be is more specific than the possible.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection-and-response sequence. It begins with the author's argument that divine command (amr) necessarily implies divine will (irada). The proof uses a reductio: if God commanded Abu Jahl to believe but willed his disbelief, that would entail two contradictory requirements—impossible. The author then notes that knowledge ('ilm) is exempted from this entailment because knowledge is merely informative, not demanding. The Ash'ari object, denying that every command implies will. They argue that will only attaches to what is known to occur and is possible. Will cannot contradict knowledge because its essence is specification (takhsis), which applies only to what actually comes to be (mutajaddid). If the commander knows the commanded will never occur, willing it is absurd, as will would have no object. The author responds by comparing will to power (qudra): if will were like power in being attached to all possibles, it could attach to the contrary of knowledge. But will is more specific—it attaches only to what actually comes to be, not to all possibles. Thus the Ash'ari objection does not refute the original argument; it only clarifies the scope of will.

Key Terms
الأمر
الإرادة
العلم
التخصيص
المتجدد
المقدور
الممكن
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari completely deny any connection between command and will.
Correction: The Ash'ari affirm that command implies will, but only if the commander knows the commanded will occur. They reject the implication when the contrary is known.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari objection, why is it impossible for God to will a commanded act that He knows will never occur?
A) Because will requires a real object, and what never occurs has no object for will to attach to.
B) Because God cannot command what He knows will not happen.
C) Because will is more general than power and can attach to impossibles.
D) Because command itself necessitates existence.

Key Takeaway
Divine command implies divine will only when the commander knows the commanded act will come to be; otherwise, will would lack an object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know Allah has commanded you to believe and obey. But are there areas in your life where you suspect He may not will your success because He knows you will fail? How does that affect your sincerity? What would it mean for you to strive as if both command and will are aligned for you?
?
Consider a time you commanded or expected something from someone while knowing they would not do it — did that make your command genuine? How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge and will challenge your view of Allah's commands to humanity? Are you like Abu Jahl, hearing the command but assuming it won't apply to you?
?
The lesson shows that will only applies to what actually comes to be. Look at your spiritual goals: are they just wishes, or does your effort indicate that you truly will them into being? What needs to change in your daily actions to make your intentions align with what you know Allah wills for you?

The Secret of Kasb: God's Will and Human Acts
The author answers an objection by distinguishing two aspects of the act and resolving divine will regarding sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the objection by distinguishing the act as coming-to-be and specification from the act as acquisition, showing that God wills all events in terms of existence but not in terms of their moral quality.

Core Idea
God wills all events that occur, but not sin as sin.

This passage presents the final resolution to the theological puzzle of divine will and human action. The author faces a sharp objection: if what is contrary to God's knowledge cannot happen, how can He will it? The answer lies in a crucial distinction between two aspects of any act—its existence and its moral character. This distinction refutes both the Qadariyya and the Jabriyya.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where an opponent argues that if something contrary to God's knowledge is impossible, then it cannot be willed. Why does this seem problematic?
The objection tries to force a contradiction between God's will and His knowledge.
2
Grasp the answer: two aspects of the act
The author introduces a key distinction: the act as commanded (its moral quality) is the servant's acquisition (kasb), while the act as coming-to-be and specification is from God. In which aspect is God willing?
Look for the phrases 'from the side of the servant' and 'from the side of God'.
3
Follow the application to obedience and sin
See how the author applies this distinction: an act that matches the command is willed and pleasing; one that contradicts the command is willed but not pleasing. Understand the meaning of 'willed' here.
Willed means willed in terms of existence, not moral approval.
4
Reflect on the refutation of Qadariyya and Jabriyya
The author concludes that this is the secret that deflates the claims of both extreme sects. How does this view preserve both divine sovereignty and human responsibility?
Consider why this middle path is called the secret of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال بعض المخالفين: إن خلاف المعلوم غير مقدور وهو أعم، فخلاف المعلوم كيف يكون مرادا وهو أخص؟ ثم نقول: من رأيي الآمر بالشيء لا يكون مريدا المأمور به من حيث إنه مأمور به قط سواء كان المأمور به طاعة أو غيره، وقد علم الآمر حصولها، وسواء كان الأمر بخلاف ذلك فإن جهة المأمور به هو كسب المأمور، وقد بينا أن ذلك أخص وصف للفعل سمي به المرء عابدا مطيعا مصليا وصائما مزكيا حاجا غازيا مجاهدا، والفعل من هذا الوجه لا ينسب إلى الباري تعالى، فلا يكون مريدا له من هذا الوجه بل ينسب إليه من حيث التجدد والتخصيص، وما لم يكن الفعل فعلا للمريد لا يكون مرادا له، فما كان من جهة العبد من الذي سميناه كسبا وقع على وفق العلم والأمر كان مرادا ومرضيا؛ أعني مرادا بالتجدد والتخصيص مرضيا بالثناء والثواب والجزاء، وما وقع على وفق العلم وخلاف الأمر كان مرادا غير مرضي؛ أعني مرادا بالتجدد غير مرضي بالذم والعقاب. وهذا هو سر هذه المسألة، ومن اطلع عليه استهان بتهويلات القدرية وتمويهات الجبرية، فعلى هذا لم يكن الباري تعالى مريدا للشرور والمعاصي والقبائح من حيث إنها شرور ومعاصي وقبائح، ولا هو مريد للخيرات والطاعات والمحاسن من حيث إنها كذلك، بل هو مريد لكل ما تجدد وحدث في العالم من حيث إنها متخصصة بالوجود دون العدم، ومتقدرة بأقدار دون أقدار، ومتأقتة بأوقات دون أوقات، ثم ذلك الموجود قد يقع منتسبا إلى استطاعة العبد كسبا على وفق الأمر فسمي طاعة مرضية؛ أي مقبولة بالثناء عاجلا والثواب آجلا، وقد يقع على خلاف الأمر فيسمى معصية غير مرضية؛ أي مردود بالذم عاجلا وبالعقاب آجلا.
Then some opponents said: 'That which is contrary to what is known is not possible, and it is more general [in scope], so how can that which is contrary to what is known be willed, given that it is more specific?' Then we say: From my view, the commander of something does not will the commanded thing inasmuch as it is commanded at all, whether the commanded thing is obedience or something else, while the commander knows its occurrence; and even if the command is contrary to that, for the aspect of the commanded thing is the acquisition (kasb) of the commanded. And we have shown that that is the most specific attribute of the act by which a person is called a worshiper, obedient, praying, fasting, giving alms, performing pilgrimage, raiding, striving. And the act in this aspect is not attributed to the Exalted Creator, so He is not willing it in this aspect; rather it is attributed to Him in terms of coming-to-be (tajaddud) and specification (takhsis). And as long as the act is not an act of the willer, it is not willed by him. So what comes from the side of the servant that we have called acquisition (kasb) and occurs in accordance with knowledge and command is willed and pleasing—meaning willed in terms of coming-to-be and specification, pleasing with praise and reward and recompense. And what occurs in accordance with knowledge and contrary to command is willed but not pleasing—meaning willed in terms of coming-to-be, not pleasing with blame and punishment. This is the secret of this issue, and whoever understands it will think little of the horrific claims of the Qadariyya and the deceptions of the Jabriyya. According to this, the Exalted Creator is not willing evils, sins, and ugly acts inasmuch as they are evils, sins, and ugly acts; nor is He willing goods, obedience, and beautiful acts inasmuch as they are so. Rather He wills everything that comes to be and occurs in the world inasmuch as they are specified with existence rather than non-existence, and quantified with measures rather than other measures, and timed with times rather than other times. Then that existing thing may occur attributed to the servant's capacity as acquisition in accordance with command, so it is called pleasing obedience—i.e., accepted with immediate praise and future reward; and it may occur contrary to command, so it is called displeasing disobedience—i.e., rejected with immediate blame and future punishment.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence that resolves the entire discussion. A second opponent objects: if what is contrary to known is not possible, then how can it be willed? The author answers by distinguishing two aspects of any act. The act as commanded (i.e., its moral quality like obedience) is from the servant's acquisition (kasb), and in that aspect it is not attributed to God, so He does not will it in that aspect. The act as coming-to-be (tajaddud) and specification (takhsis) is from God, and in that aspect He wills it. Thus God wills all events insofar as they exist, but He does not will them in their moral character (sin or obedience). The moral character arises from the servant's kasb relative to the command. The author concludes that this is the secret of the issue that refutes both Qadariyya (who deny God's will over evil) and Jabriyya (who attribute evil to God's will in the sense of moral approval).

Key Terms
الكسب
الطاعة
المعصية
التجدد
التخصيص
المرضي
القدرية
الجبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills everything that happens, then He wills evil in the same sense He wills good.
Correction: God wills all events in terms of their existence (tajaddud and takhsis), but He does not will the moral quality of sin; that arises from the servant's kasb relative to His command.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in what sense is an act of disobedience (ma'siya) willed by God?
A) As an act of disobedience, God wills it because it fulfills His command.
B) Only in terms of its coming-to-be and specification, not as disobedience.
C) God does not will it at all, since it is evil.
D) God wills it as a punishment for the servant.

Key Takeaway
God wills all events in terms of their existence, but the moral quality of acts comes from the servant's acquisition (kasb) relative to the divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you acknowledge that God willed its existence but not its sinfulness, and that you alone are responsible for the sin? How does this realization affect your repentance and your view of God's justice? Will you stop blaming God for your failures and take ownership of your choices?
?
The passage says that the act in terms of acquisition (kasb) is attributed to you, not to God. Does this truth make you more careful about every action you undertake? Are you willing to face the fact that you are the one who makes the act good or evil by your intention and choice?
?
If God wills all events but does not will sin as sin, then your disobedience is still under His sovereign plan. How does this shape your humility before Him? Will you trust His wisdom even when you cannot understand why He allowed a particular sin to occur in your life?

Why Divine Will and Command Are Not Always the Same
Argues that divine will and command are not necessarily co-extensive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine command (amr) and divine will (irada) are not necessarily co-extensive, and how the Ash'ari framework distinguishes between Allah's creation of acts and human acquisition.

Core Idea
Command and will can diverge: Allah wills all acts as created, but commands only the morally good.

The relationship between what Allah wills and what He commands is a subtle but crucial point in Ash'ari theology. In this passage, the author argues that divine will attaches to the act as a temporal event (which Allah creates), while divine command attaches to the moral quality acquired by the human. This allows for acts that Allah wills (like evil deeds) but does not command, and commands He issues (like belief) that He wills for the believer but not for the disbeliever. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counterfactual about human power
The author starts with a hypothetical: if humans had no power, acts would be entirely from Allah with no moral labels. This sets up the need for human acquisition to allow moral responsibility.
This counterfactual shows that moral distinction requires human contribution.
2
Trace the attribution of actions to Allah as temporal events
All actions, in their temporal origination, are from Allah and directed towards cosmic order and good. So from one angle, all acts are pure good.
This is the basis for the Ash'ari view that Allah creates all acts, including evil, but evil is not in the aspect of creation.
3
Examine the Quranic verses about Allah's will for good
Verses like 'Allah desires ease for you' cannot mean that Allah eternally wills ease for every person, because some people experience hardship. So they must be interpreted as praise or reward.
This shows that 'will' in the Quran is not always the eternal attribute of irada.
4
Derive the conclusion about will and command
The eternal will only attaches to actions as temporally originated (which are all from Allah). Command attaches to the moral aspect attributed to humans. Therefore, will and command are not bound together.
This resolves the problem of evil: Allah wills the act's existence but does not command its sinful aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولولا قدرة العبد في الشيء لكان الفعل فعلا متجددا مختصا بما خصصته الإرادة من غير أن يقال : هو خير أو شر إيمان أو كفر، فالأفعال كلها من حيث تجددها واختصاصها مرداة للباري تعالى وهي متوجهة إلى نظام في الوجود وصلاح العالم، وذلك هو الخير المحض، وكانت وجوهها إلى الخير، وظهورها من الخير وكان بيده الخير، وكلما ورد في القرآن من إرادة الخير المخصوص بأفعال العباد مثل قوله : ( يريد الله بكم اليسر ولا يريد بكم العسر ولكن يريد ليظهركم إلى غير ذلك ، فهو محمول على أحد معنيين ؛ إما ثناء ومدح في الحال، وإما على ثواب ونعمة في المال، وإلا فالإرادة الأزلية لا تتعلق إلا بما هو متجدد من حيث إنه متجدد، فلا متجدد إلا وهو فعل للباري تعالى من حيث إنه متجدد وذلك لا ينسب إلى العبد كما بينا في خلق الأعمال، فاختصت الإرادة بأفعال الله سبحانه على الحقيقة دون الوجوه التي تنسب إلى العبد، واختص الأمر بأفعال العباد حقيقة دون الوجوه التي تنسب إلى الحق تعالى، فلم يجب تلازم الأمر والإرادة.
Were it not for the human's power in the thing, the act would be a temporally originated act specifically determined by what the will specified, without it being said: it is good or evil, faith or disbelief. So all actions, in terms of their temporal origination and specification, are attributed to the Exalted Lord, and they are directed towards an order in existence and the rectification of the world, and that is pure good. And their aspects were towards good, and their emergence was from good, and in His hand is good. And whatever has come in the Quran regarding the will of specific good in relation to the acts of servants, such as His saying: 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship for you, but He desires to purify you' and so on, it is interpreted in one of two meanings: either as praise and commendation in the present, or as reward and blessing in the future. Otherwise, the eternal will does not attach except to what is temporally originated inasmuch as it is temporally originated. So nothing temporally originated exists except that it is an act of the Exalted Lord inasmuch as it is temporally originated, and that is not attributed to the servant as we have explained in the creation of actions. Thus, the will is specifically attached to the acts of Allah, exalted, in truth, without the aspects that are attributed to the servant. And the command is specifically attached to the acts of the servants in truth, without the aspects that are attributed to the Truth, Exalted. So the mutual entailment of command and will is not necessary.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing that divine command (amr) and divine will (irada) are not necessarily co-extensive. The author begins with a counterfactual: if the human had no power, then every act would be directly from Allah, and moral distinctions would not apply. But in reality, acts are attributed to both Allah (as temporally originated) and humans (as acquired). The author then interprets Quranic verses about Allah's will for good in human acts as either praise or reward, not as His eternal will. The eternal will only attaches to acts as temporally originated entities, which are all Allah's creation. So will is truly about the act as such, while command is about the moral aspect attributed to humans. Hence, command and will do not necessarily entail each other.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
تجدّد (tajaddud)
خلق الأعمال (khalq al-a'mal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine command always implies divine will for the commanded act, and vice versa.
Correction: The Ash'ari distinction shows that Allah can command an act (e.g., faith) without willing it for a particular person (e.g., a disbeliever), because command attaches to the moral aspect while will attaches to the act's existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when Quranic verses speak of Allah's will for good in human acts, what do they actually mean?
A) They mean Allah eternally wills good for everyone.
B) They are either praise in the present or reward in the future.
C) They show that Allah only wills good.
D) They are metaphorical and have no real meaning.

Key Takeaway
Divine will and command are not necessarily co-extensive: will attaches to acts as created events, command attaches to their moral quality acquired by humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own sins, do you truly believe that Allah willed them as created events but did not command them? How does this recognition change your view of Allah's justice and your personal accountability? Will you now repent with the understanding that your sin was created by Allah but acquired by you?
?
The passage states that every act, as a temporally originated event, is 'pure good' directed toward the order of the cosmos. How does this confront your feeling that some events in your life—loss, illness, failure—are purely evil or meaningless? Can you accept that Allah's will for them is good even when you cannot see it? What would it take for you to trust this truth in your next trial?
?
If you realize that Allah commands obedience but does not always will it for a particular person, how does that affect your prayers for guidance and steadfastness? Do you rely on Allah's will to enable your obedience, or do you presume upon His command as if it guarantees His willing? Starting today, will you beg Allah to will for you what He commands, knowing that the two can diverge?

Refuting the Polytheists' Misuse of Divine Will
Refutes polytheists' misuse of 'will' as 'command' and explains the true meaning of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the polytheists misused the term 'will' to excuse their shirk, and how the Qur'an refutes them by distinguishing between 'will' and 'command'.

Core Idea
The polytheists wrongly equated Allah's will with His command to excuse shirk.

In this passage, Imam al-Shahrastani addresses a common polytheist argument: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' They used this to claim that their shirk was divinely sanctioned. The author explains that they deliberately confused 'will' with 'command'. Allah Himself refutes them in the Qur'an, showing that divine will pertains to actual existence and disposal, not merely to commands. Let's explore this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the homonymy
Recognize that in Arabic, words like 'will' and 'command' can be used interchangeably through homonymy, leading to potential confusion.
This is a linguistic point that the polytheists exploited.
2
Identify the polytheists' argument
Read the verse they cited: 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners...' and note how they interpreted 'will' as 'command' to excuse their shirk.
3
See the Qur'anic challenge
Notice that Allah challenges them to produce knowledge or scripture supporting their interpretation, and calls their claim mere assumption and lies.
4
Grasp the refutation
Understand that Allah refutes them by affirming His will as specification for existence and disposal of affairs, not as a command that would permit shirk.
5
Apply the conclusive argument
Reflect on the verse 'To Allah belongs the conclusive argument. If He had willed, He would have guided you all.' This shows that if Allah had willed guidance, He would have ensured it, but He did not, so their excuse fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يرد الأمر بمعنى الإرادة والإرادة بمعنى الآمر فيكون الإطلاق باشتراك في اللفظ، فكان المشركون تمسكوا بهذا اللفظ حيث أخبر التنزيل عنهم : ( سَيَقُولُ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا لَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْرَكْنَا وَلَا ءَابَاؤُنَا وَلَا حَرَّمْنَا مِن شَيْءٍ ) أرادوا بذلك المشيئة بمعنى الأمر، وعن هذا طالبهم بإخراج العلم بذلك وإظهار الحجة عليهم من كتبهم وقال : ( إِن يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ وَإِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا يَخْرُصُونَ ) إذ لم يرد بذلك أمر، ولا وجدوا في كتبهم بذلك تكليفا فرد عليهم بإثبات المشيئة؛ بمعنى التخصيص بالوجود والتصريف للأمور، فقال تعالى : ( قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحَجَّةُ الْبَالِغَةُ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ ).
And it is possible that 'command' is used in the sense of 'will' and 'will' in the sense of 'commander', so the application is through verbal homonymy. Thus the polytheists clung to this wording when the Revelation reported about them: 'Those who associate partners will say, "If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our fathers, nor would we have prohibited anything."' They meant by that the will in the sense of command. And for this reason, He challenged them to bring forth knowledge of that and to show the proof against them from their books, and He said: 'They follow nothing but assumption, and they are only lying.' Since He did not intend by that a command, nor did they find in their books a prescription regarding that. So He refuted them by affirming the will in the sense of specification for existence and the disposal of affairs, saying: 'Say: "To Allah belongs the conclusive argument. If He had willed, He would have guided you all."'
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a polytheist argument. The author first notes that occasionally words like 'command' and 'will' are used interchangeably via homonymy. The polytheists exploited this, citing the verse 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' to claim that if Allah had willed otherwise, He would have commanded otherwise, thus excusing their shirk. The author explains that the polytheists meant 'will' in the sense of 'command'. But Allah challenged them to produce knowledge or scripture supporting that. The Qur'anic response is that they only follow assumptions. Then Allah refutes them by affirming that His will is about actual specification and disposal of affairs, not command. The conclusive argument is that if Allah had willed (i.e., ordained) guidance for all, He would have guided them, but He did not. Thus, their excuse fails.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashi'a)
أمر (amr)
اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)
حجة بالغة (hujja baligha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the polytheists' use of 'if Allah willed' is a valid excuse for their shirk.
Correction: The author shows that the polytheists misinterpreted 'will' as 'command'. Allah's will is about actual existence and disposal, not a command that would permit shirk. The conclusive argument is that if Allah had willed guidance for all, He would have guided them.

Checkpoint
What did the polytheists mean by 'mashi'a' in their statement?
A) Divine specification for existence.
B) Divine command.
C) Divine pleasure.
D) Divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The polytheists excused shirk by confusing Allah's will with His command, but Allah refutes them by showing that His will is about actual existence and guidance, not a command that would excuse disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used Allah's will as an excuse to avoid taking responsibility for a sin or shortcoming, similar to the polytheists? How does this passage expose the flaw in that thinking?
?
When you say 'if Allah wills,' do you truly understand it as a reference to His absolute decree and disposal, or have you unintentionally conflated it with a command that makes you passive? What is one area of your life where you need to replace passive reliance on 'will' with active obedience to 'command'?
?
The polytheists hid behind a linguistic confusion. Where might you be hiding behind a misunderstanding of Allah's attributes to avoid a hard truth about your own faith or actions? What will you do today to confront that?

Divergence of Command and Will: Al-Ash'ari's Examples
Provides three examples where command and will diverge, from al-Ash'ari and his school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari uses concrete examples to demonstrate that divine command and divine will are not necessarily aligned.

Core Idea
Command and will can point in opposite directions.

Al-Ash'ari and his school provide vivid examples to illustrate a subtle theological point: what Allah commands is not always what He wills. These examples help clarify the distinction between the attributes of command (amr) and will (irada). As you read the passage, notice how each scenario separates the commanded action from the intended outcome.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the passage opens with a claim and then lists examples.
The final example is incomplete in the source due to OCR issues.
2
Identify the first example
The first example involves a prophet commanding twenty deeds but intending compliance with ten. Understand why the command is for twenty while the will is for ten.
This illustrates command contrary to will.
3
Identify the second and third examples
The second example is the Night of the Ascension (Mi'raj), where fifty prayers were commanded but reduced to five. The third involves a master commanding slaves while willing their disobedience.
The third example may feel less divine but follows the same logic.
4
Look at the incomplete fourth example
The fourth example cites Allah's command to Ibrahim to sacrifice his son while willing it not to occur. The text breaks off, but the intended point is clear: command and will are distinct.
This is the most significant example for theological doctrine.
5
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: What does this imply about the relationship between Allah's attributes? Does a command necessarily express His will? The examples show that commands can serve other purposes (e.g., testing, demonstrating).
This connects to the wider Ash'ari position on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمهل أبو الحسن وأصحابه أمثلة في جواز تعلق الإرادة على خلاف الأمر؛ منها أن نبيا لو علم من أحد الأمة أنه لو أمر بعشر خصال من الخير توانى فيها، ولو أمر بعشرين خصلة ثم حط عشرة منها عنه لم يقصر، فيأمره بالعشرين على إرادة امتثال العشرة، فهو مأمور بخلاف المراد ومراد بخلاف المأمور، ومثل هذا قد وقع ليلة المعراج؛ حيث أمر النبي بخمسين صلاة ثم ردت إلى الخمس، ومنها أن رجلا لو اشتكى عند السلطان من عبيده بعصيانهم عليه وقلة مبالاتهم فيستحضرهم الملك فيأمرهم المالك بشيء فهو يريد مخالفتهم إياه تصديقًا لمقاله، فذلك أمر على خلاف الإرادة، ومنها أن الرب تعالى أمر خليله إبراهيم بذبح الولد وهو يريد أن لا يحصل؛ إذ لو أراد الحص.
And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his students have cited examples concerning the permissibility of the will being attached contrary to the command. Among them: that if a prophet knew of a member of his community that if he commanded him with ten virtuous deeds he would be negligent in them, but if he commanded him with twenty deeds then reduced ten of them he would not be negligent, then he commands him with the twenty intending compliance with the ten. So he is commanded contrary to what is willed, and what is willed is contrary to what is commanded. And something like this occurred on the Night of the Ascension, when the Prophet was commanded with fifty prayers, then they were reduced to five. And among them: that if a man complained before a ruler about his slaves' disobedience and lack of regard, then the king summons them and the master commands them with something while he wills their opposition to him in confirmation of his statement. So that is a command contrary to the will. And among them: that the Exalted Lord commanded His friend Abraham to slaughter his son while He willed that it not occur; because if He had willed its occurrence...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of illustrative examples from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers (the Ash'ari school) to demonstrate that it is possible for the divine will (irada) to be attached contrary to a command (amr). Three examples are given: (1) A prophet, knowing that commanding ten good deeds would cause negligence but commanding twenty then reducing to ten would bring compliance, commands twenty intending the ten; thus the command is for twenty but the will is for ten. (2) On the Night of the Ascension (Mi'raj), the Prophet was commanded with fifty prayers, but the effective will was for five, as they were reduced. (3) A ruler whose servant complains about his slaves' disobedience may command the slaves to do something, but wills their disobedience to confirm the complaint. (4) The most significant: Allah commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice his son but willed that it not actually occur; otherwise it would have happened. This shows that command and will can differ. The third example is incomplete in the text, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)
خلاف الأمر (khilaf al-amr)
امتثال (imtithal)
ليلة المعراج (laylat al-mi'raj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A divine command always indicates what Allah wills to happen.
Correction: The examples from al-Ash'ari show that commands can be issued for purposes other than bringing about the commanded act, such as testing or demonstrating obedience. Thus command and will are not necessarily aligned.

Checkpoint
What is the common theme of the examples cited by al-Ash'ari?
A) Allah's will is always fulfilled in the world.
B) Command and will can be directed at opposite outcomes.
C) Prophets always know what is willed.
D) Commands are only for testing.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's examples demonstrate that divine command (amr) and divine will (irada) are distinct attributes that can sometimes point in opposite directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever obeyed a command of Allah outwardly while inwardly wishing it were different? How does the distinction between command and will help you trust that His command is good even when your will struggles? Will you now choose to align your will with His commands, surrendering your understanding to His wisdom?
?
Consider a time when you gave an instruction to someone (a child, student, or employee) knowing they might not fully obey. Did your intention match your words? How does the example of the master commanding his slaves to disobey challenge you to be honest about your intentions? What step will you take today to align your commands with what you truly will?
?
If Allah's command to Ibrahim was not because He willed the sacrifice, but to test his ultimate submission, what does that reveal about the purpose of commands in your life? Can you identify a command of Allah that seems difficult? Will you embrace it as a test of your love and obedience, regardless of the outcome?

Divine Will vs Command: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Refuting the Mu'tazila claim that divine will means command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will (mashi'a) and command (amr) according to Sunni theology, and how the Mu'tazila conflation is refuted by Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Not everything Allah wills is commanded; His will is broader than His command.

The Mu'tazila attempted to reinterpret divine will as merely a command, but this creates theological problems. The Ash'ari response uses Quranic verses to show that Allah's will encompasses both command and permission, and that His will is not identical to His command. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The passage begins by stating that the Mu'tazila meant by mashi'a the sense of command. Understand their position.
2
Examine the refutation method
The author challenges them to produce knowledge from their own scriptures, citing Quran 6:116 about following conjecture.
Notice the use of 'they follow nothing but conjecture'.
3
Contrast the correct meaning
The correct meaning of mashi'a is specification for existence and disposal of affairs. This is supported by Quran 6:149.
The verse 'If He had willed, He would have guided you all' shows will is not limited to command.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why distinguishing will from command is important for understanding divine justice and predestination.
This distinction is crucial in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرادوا بذلك المشيئة بمعنى الأمر، وعن هذا طالبهم بإخراج العلم بذلك وإظهار الحجة عليهم من كتبهم وقال: {إِن يَتَّبِعُونَ إِلَّا الظَّنَّ وَإِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا تَخْرُصُونَ} [الأنعام: 116] إذ لم يرد بذلك أمر، ولا وجدوا في كتبهم بذلك تكليفا فرد عليهم بإثبات المشيئة؛ بمعنى التخصيص بالوجود والتصريف للأمور، فقال تعالى: {قُلْ فَلِلَّهِ الْحَجَّةُ الْبَلِغَةُ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَنَكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ} [الأنعام: 149].
They meant by that mashi'a (المشيئة) in the sense of command (الأمر). And concerning this, He called upon them to produce knowledge of that and to manifest the proof against them from their books, and He said: {They follow nothing but conjecture, and they are only lying} [Al-An'am:116] since He did not mean by that a command, and they did not find in their books any obligation for that. So He refuted them by affirming mashi'a (المشيئة) in the sense of specification (التخصيص) for existence and disposal of affairs. He said: {Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument. If He had willed, He would have guided you all} [Al-An'am:149].
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position regarding the meaning of mashi'a (will). The Mu'tazila had interpreted mashi'a as meaning 'command' (amr). The author responds by quoting the Quranic verse {They follow nothing but conjecture} to argue that this interpretation is not based on firm knowledge. He then points out that the Mu'tazila cannot find any scriptural command that would support their view. Instead, the author affirms that mashi'a means 'specification of existence and disposal of affairs' (takhsis bi al-wujud wa al-tasrif li al-umur). He then cites the verse {Say: To Allah belongs the conclusive argument} to show that if Allah had willed, He would have guided all, but He does not, which indicates that His will is not identical to His command.

Key Terms

الأمر
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's will is exactly His command.
Correction: Allah's will encompasses both command and permission; He may will something without commanding it.

Checkpoint

A) Divine specification
B) Command
C) Power
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
Divine will (mashi'a) is not synonymous with command (amr); it is a broader attribute of specification and disposal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila forced their own logic onto Allah's attributes. When have you tried to confine Allah to your own human sense of 'fairness'? What would it cost you to accept that His will is not what you would command?
?
If Allah wills both guidance and misguidance, then your own faith is entirely from Him. Does that humble you? Or does it make you secretly proud that He chose you? How should this reshape your gratitude?
?
The verse says, 'If He had willed, He would have guided you all.' Why has He not? And what does that demand from you today — to whom will you extend mercy instead of judgment?

Examples of Command Contrary to Will
Al-Ash'ari provides four examples of command contrary to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Islamic theology, divine command (amr) can be distinct from divine will (irada), as illustrated by examples from al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Command and will are not always the same; Allah can command what He does not will.

Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers provided vivid examples to demonstrate that the divine command (amr) can be contrary to the divine will (irada). This distinction is crucial for understanding how Allah's commands relate to His will and how some commands may have underlying wisdom beyond immediate obedience. In this passage, we examine several scenarios that clarify this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the examples speak for themselves first.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the principle before listing examples.
2
Identify the underlying principle
What principle is al-Ash'ari trying to prove? Look for the phrase 'جواز تعلق الإرادة على خلاف الأمر' – the permissibility of will attaching contrary to command.
This principle is the key to understanding the examples.
3
Examine each example
Go through each example one by one: the prophet's tactic, the Mi'raj incident, the slave scenario, and Ibrahim's sacrifice. For each, identify what was commanded and what was willed.
The examples show different contexts: human interaction, prophetic history, and divine command.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this distinction matters. It prevents misunderstanding Allah's commands and affirms His wisdom and freedom in willing.
This lesson guards against the misconception that command always implies will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تمهل أبو الحسن وأصحابه أمثلة في جواز تعلق الإرادة على خلاف الأمر؛ منها أن نبيا لو علم من أحد الأمة أنه لو أمر بعشر خصال من الخير توانى فيها، ولو أمر بعشرين خصلة ثم حط عشرة منها عنه لم يقصر، فيأمره بالعشرين على إرادة امتثال العشرة، فهو مأمور بخلاف المراد ومراد بخلاف المأمور، ومثل هذا قد وقع ليلة المعراج؛ حيث أمر النبي بخمسين صلاة ثم ردت إلى الخمس، ومنها أن رجلا لو اشتكى عند السلطان من عبيده بعصيانهم عليه وقلة مبالاتهم فيستحضرهم الملك فيأمرهم المالك بشيء فهو يريد مخالفتهم إياه تصديقًا لمقاله، فذلك أمر على خلاف الإرادة، ومنها أن الرب تعالى أمر خليله إبراهيم بذبح الولد وهو يريد أن لا يحصل؛ إذ لو أراد الحصل.
Abu al-Hasan [al-Ash'ari] and his companions gave examples of the permissibility of will (الإرادة) attaching contrary to command (الأمر). Among them: that a prophet, if he knew that if he commanded a nation with ten virtuous qualities they would be negligent, but if he commanded them with twenty qualities and then reduced ten of them, they would not fall short, he would command them with the twenty intending the compliance with ten. Thus he is commanded contrary to what is willed, and what is willed is contrary to what is commanded. An example similar to this occurred on the Night of the Ascension (المعراج), when the Prophet was commanded with fifty prayers, but they were reduced to five. Another example: if a man complained to a ruler about his slaves' disobedience and lack of concern, the king summons them and the master commands them with something while intending that they oppose him to confirm his words. That is a command contrary to will. Another example: the Lord Almighty commanded His friend Ibrahim to sacrifice his son while He intended that it not happen; for if He intended its occurrence [then He would have willed it].
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents examples from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers to illustrate that the divine will (irada) can be different from the divine command (amr). The first example involves a prophet who, knowing the weakness of his people, commands them with more than he intends in order to secure compliance with a lesser number. The second example refers to the event of the Mi'raj (Ascension) where the Prophet was commanded with fifty prayers but the actual will was for five. The third example is a scenario where a master commands his slaves to do something but intends them to disobey to prove a point to the ruler. The final example is the command to Ibrahim to sacrifice his son, while Allah intended that it not actually occur. These examples show that the commanded action can be contrary to what is willed, thus distinguishing between the two concepts.

الأمر
الامتثال
المخالفة
المعراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might mistakenly think that Allah's command always reflects His will.
Correction: These examples show that command and will can differ; Allah may command something for a reason other than its actual occurrence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Divine command and divine will are distinct; Allah's commands may serve purposes beyond the commanded act itself.

Interpreting 'He Does Not Approve' in Q 39:7
Interpretation of the verse with analogy and argument from opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's approval/disapproval as a judgment of value versus His creative will, and how the verse 'He does not approve for His servants disbelief' is interpreted in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
God's disapproval is a judgment of evil, not an act of will.

This lesson examines a key verse often cited in debates about divine will and human action. The author clarifies that 'does not approve' refers to God's judgment of disbelief as a bad religion, not to His creating it. By analyzing the opposition between approval and displeasure, he shows that these terms are about praise and blame, reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Focus on the author's interpretation of the verse and his supporting argument.
Notice the analogy of buying a defective slave.
2
Understand the analogy
The author compares God's disapproval of disbelief to a buyer rejecting a defective slave. This illustrates that 'does not approve' means 'does not accept as good' rather than 'does not will.'
3
Analyze the argument from opposites
The author argues that approval and displeasure are opposites. Since displeasure implies blame and punishment, approval implies praise and reward. Thus, the verse refers to recompense, not creative will.
This reinforces that God's disapproval is a judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله تعالى : ( وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ ) أي : لا يرضى لهم دينا وشرعاً فإنه وخيم العاقبة كثير المضرة، كمن يشتري عبداً معيبًا فيقول له : لا أرضى هذا لك عبدا، ومما يتقوى بهذا المعنى أن الرضا والسخط يتقابلان تقابل التضاد، ثم السخط لم يكن محمولًا إلا على ذم في الحال، وعقاب في المال، كذلك الرضا محمول على الثناء في الحال وثواب في المال.
As for His saying 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Q 39:7), meaning: He does not approve for them as a religion and a law, for indeed it is evil in consequence, great in harm, like one who buys a defective slave and says to him: 'I do not approve of this for you as a slave.' And among what strengthens this meaning is that approval and displeasure are opposite to each other by the opposition of contrariety. Then displeasure is not attributed except to blame in the present and punishment in the hereafter. Likewise, approval is attributed to praise in the present and reward in the hereafter.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed interpretation of the verse 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Q 39:7). The author explains that 'does not approve' means that He does not approve of disbelief as a religion and law, because it is harmful and evil in outcome. He gives an analogy: someone who buys a defective slave and says 'I do not approve of this for you as a slave' – meaning he does not accept it as a condition. The point is that God's disapproval of disbelief is not an act of volition but a judgment of its badness. The author then strengthens this by noting that approval and displeasure are opposites. Displeasure, when applied to God, indicates blame in this world and punishment in the next. Similarly, approval indicates praise now and reward in the hereafter. Thus, the verse is not about God's will but about His judgment and recompense.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that it refers to His disapproval of it as a religion, not His creative will.

Key Takeaway
The verse means God judges disbelief as evil and worthy of punishment, not that He does not will its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The analogy of the defective slave shows God rejecting disbelief as unsuitable. In what areas of your faith have you compromised, treating them as 'good enough' even though God has declared them unsuitable? What will you do today to align your practice with what He truly approves?
?
If God's approval and displeasure are opposites, and displeasure leads to blame and punishment, have you ever considered that your small, tolerated sins accumulate a weight of divine displeasure? How can you live in constant awareness that every action is either approved or disapproved by the One who will reward or punish you?

The Single Divine Will and Its Multiple Manifestations
Ja'far al-Sadiq's distinction and the author's classification of the divine will's names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the one divine will takes different names (approval, displeasure, etc.) based on its relation to different objects, and how Ja'far al-Sadiq's distinction between 'willed by us' and 'willed from us' resolves apparent contradictions in Quranic verses about God's will.

Core Idea
God's will is one; its names vary by relation to what is willed.

In this passage, the author addresses a key theological puzzle: how can the same God will both good and evil, both reward and punishment? He cites the insightful saying of Ja'far al-Sadiq that distinguishes between what God wills by us (His commands) and what He wills from us (His knowledge). This leads to a detailed classification showing that the single divine will is called by different names depending on its object. Let's explore this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the author starts with a Quranic verse and then cites Ja'far al-Sadiq.
2
Identify Ja'far's distinction
Find the key saying: 'God willed something by us and willed something from us.' Understand what each refers to (commands vs. knowledge).
3
Trace the classification of names
The author shows that when the will relates to reward it is called approval, etc. Notice the five relations: reward, punishment, knowledge, command, and absolute creation.
Create a mental list of these relations and their corresponding names.
4
Apply to human actions
Read the final part where the author applies this classification to human actions, noting the two possible ways to understand God's will regarding them.
This ties back to the initial puzzle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ( وَاللهُ يُرِيدُ أَن يَتُوبَ عَلَيْكُمْ ) وسائر الآيات في الإرادة محمولة على كلمة ذكرها الصادق جعفر بن محمد رضي الله عنه : إن الله تعالى أراد بنا شيئًا وأراد منا شيئًا ، فما أراده بنا أظهره لنا ، وما أراده منا طواه عنا، فما بالنا نشتغل بما أراده منا عما أراده بنا ومعنى ذلك أنه أراد بنا ما أمرنا به، وأراد منا ما علمه، فكانت الإرادة واحدة يختلف حكمها باختلاف وجه تعلقها بالمراد، وهي إذا تعلقت بثواب سميت رضا ومحبة وإذا تعلقت بعقاب سمیت سخطا وغضبا، وكذلك إذا تعلقت بالمراد على وجه تعلق العلم به قيل أراد منه ما علم، وإذا تعلقت بالمراد على وجه تعلق الأمر قيل : أراد به ما أمر، وإذا تعلقت بالصنع مطلقا بالتخصيص والتعيين من غير التفات إلى كسب العبد حتى يكون أراد منه وأراده به قيل : أراد الكائنات بأسرها، ولم يقل : أراد منها وأراد بها ، بل أرادها على ما هي عليه من التجدد والتخصيص بالوجود دون العدم. فإذا أفعال العباد من حيث إنها أفعالهم ؛ إما أن يقال : تتعلق الإرادة بها إلا على الوجه الذي ينسب إلى العباد بل على الوجه الذي انتسب إلى الخلق إيجادا وتخصيصاً، وإما أن يقال : تعلقت الإرادة بها على الوجهين المذكورين؛ أنه أراد بنا وأراد منا أراد بنا ما أمر دينا وشرعاً واعتقادًا ومذهبًا وأراد منا ما علم سابقة
And as for His saying 'And Allah wants to turn in mercy to you' (Q 4:27) and the rest of the verses concerning will, they are carried upon a statement mentioned by al-Ṣādiq Jaʿfar ibn Muḥammad (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Indeed Allah, exalted, willed something by us and willed something from us. What He willed by us He made apparent to us, and what He willed from us He concealed from us. So why do we occupy ourselves with what He willed from us over what He willed by us?' The meaning of that is that He willed by us what He commanded us, and He willed from us what He knew. So the will is one, its judgment differs according to the aspect of its relation to the object of will. When it relates to reward it is called approval and love; when it relates to punishment it is called displeasure and anger. Likewise, when it relates to the object of will under the aspect of the relation of knowledge to it, one says 'He willed from him what He knew'; when it relates to the object of will under the aspect of the relation of command, one says 'He willed by him what He commanded'; and when it relates to creation absolutely, with specification and determination, without regard to the servant's acquisition, such that it is 'willed from him' and 'willed by him', one says 'He willed all beings as a whole', and one does not say 'He willed from them' and 'willed by them', but rather He willed them as they are, in their renewal and specification to existence rather than non-existence. So the actions of the servants, insofar as they are their actions, either it is said that will relates to them only under the aspect that is attributed to the servants, but rather under the aspect that is attributed to creation, as existence and specification, or it is said that will relates to them under the two mentioned aspects: that He willed by us and willed from us; He willed by us what He commanded as religion, law, belief, and madhhab, and He willed from us what He knew previously
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins with the interpretation of the verse 'And Allah wants to turn in mercy to you' (Q 4:27) and other verses about God's will. The author cites a profound saying from Ja'far al-Sadiq: God willed something 'by us' and something 'from us'. What He willed 'by us' He made apparent (His commands), and what He willed 'from us' He concealed (His knowledge of our actions). The author then explains the meaning: God's will is one, but its judgment differs according to the aspect of its relation to the object of will. He then categorizes these relations: if the will relates to reward, it is called approval and love; if to punishment, displeasure and anger; if it relates under the aspect of knowledge, it is said 'He willed from him what He knew'; if under the aspect of command, 'He willed by him what He commanded'; if it relates to absolute creation without regard to acquisition, it is said 'He willed all beings', meaning He created them. The passage then concludes with the application to human actions: either we say God's will relates to them only as created by Him, or we say it relates both as command and as knowledge. This distinction resolves the apparent contradictions in the verses about God's will.

تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God has multiple conflicting wills (e.g., will for good and will for evil).
Correction: God's will is one; apparent conflicts arise from different relations to the object. The same will is called approval when relating to reward and displeasure when relating to punishment.

Checkpoint
According to Ja'far al-Sadiq's saying cited by the author, what does 'God willed from us' refer to?
A) His commands.
B) His knowledge of our actions.
C) His creation of our actions.
D) His approval of our actions.

Key Takeaway
The single divine will is expressed through different names depending on its relation to the object, reconciling apparently contradictory verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ja'far al-Sadiq asks why we occupy ourselves with what God wills from us (His knowledge of our actions) over what He wills by us (His commands). How much of your daily effort is consumed by worrying about what God knows about you—your past, your future, your secret sins—rather than focusing on obeying His clear commands today? What one command from this lesson can you commit to acting upon right now?
?
The passage teaches that God's will is one, but we give it different names depending on what it relates to. How often do you view God's will in your life as fragmented—sometimes loving, sometimes angry, sometimes commanding—rather than as a single, whole divine will? In what area of your life do you need to trust that God's will is unified, even when it feels contradictory?
?
The author explains that when the divine will relates to absolute creation, God wills all beings as they are, without regard to human acquisition. Does your sense of security rest on your own efforts or on the fact that everything—including your very existence—is directly willed by God? What would change if you truly lived in that conviction today?

Divine Will as Pleasure and Anger
Defines pleasure and anger as names for divine will based on object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine pleasure and anger are not independent emotions but names for God's will when directed to reward or punishment.

Core Idea
God's pleasure and anger are one will with two outcomes.

In theological discussions, terms like 'pleasure' and 'anger' can be misleading. This lesson clarifies that when applied to God, they refer to His will in relation to reward and punishment, not emotional change. The classical scholar uses a saying of Ja'far al-Sadiq to illustrate how one divine will takes different names based on its object.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining sakhaṭ and riḍā
Notice that both are defined by their consequences: blame/praise in this life and punishment/reward in the afterlife.
2
Examine the Ja'far al-Sadiq quote
It introduces two aspects of will: what God wills for us (commands) and what He wills from us (knowledge).
This sets up the idea that will is one but has different relations.
3
Understand the naming rule
The passage concludes that when will attaches to reward it is called pleasure and love, when to punishment it is called displeasure and wrath.
This is the key point: same will, different names based on object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السخط لم يكن محمولًا إلا على ذم في الحال، وعقاب في المال، كذلك الرضا محمول على الثناء في الحال وثواب في المال. وأما قوله تعالى : ( وَاللهُ يُرِيدُ أَن يَتُوبَ عَلَيْكُمْ ) وسائر الآيات في الإرادة محمولة على كلمة ذكرها الصادق جعفر بن محمد رضي الله عنه : إن الله تعالى أراد بنا شيئًا وأراد منا شيئًا ، فما أراده بنا أظهره لنا ، وما أراده منا طواه عنا، فما بالنا نشتغل بما أراده منا عما أراده بنا ومعنى ذلك أنه أراد بنا ما أمرنا به، وأراد منا ما علمه، فكانت الإرادة واحدة يختلف حكمها باختلاف وجه تعلقها بالمراد، وهي إذا تعلقت بثواب سميت رضا ،ومحبة وإذا تعلقت بعقاب سمیت سخطا وغضبا، وكذلك إذا تعلقت بالمراد على وجه تعلق العلم به قيل أراد منه ما علم، وإذا تعلقت بالمراد على وجه تعلق الأمر قيل : أراد به ما أمر، وإذا تعلقت بالصنع مطلقا بالتخصيص والتعيين من غير التفات إلى كسب العبد حتى يكون أراد منه وأراده به قيل : أراد الكائنات بأسرها، ولم يقل : أراد منها وأراد بها ، بل أرادها على ما هي عليه من التجدد والتخصيص بالوجود دون العدم.
Displeasure is not applied except to blame in the present and punishment in the afterlife. Similarly, pleasure is applied to praise in the present and reward in the afterlife. As for His saying, exalted: "And Allah wishes to turn to you" and the rest of the verses about will, they are carried on the statement that the truthful Ja'far ibn Muhammad—God be pleased with him—mentioned: "God, exalted, willed something for us and willed something from us. What He willed for us He manifested to us, and what He willed from us He concealed from us. So why do we occupy ourselves with what He willed from us instead of what He willed for us?" The meaning of that is that He willed for us what He commanded us, and He willed from us what He knew. So the divine will is one; its judgment differs according to the aspect of its attachment to the object willed. When it attaches to reward it is called pleasure and love, and when it attaches to punishment it is called displeasure and wrath. Likewise, when it attaches to the object willed in the manner of the attachment of knowledge, it is said: He willed from it what He knew. When it attaches to the object willed in the manner of the attachment of command, it is said: He willed for it what He commanded. And when it attaches to creation absolutely, with specification and determination, without regard to the servant's acquisition, even becoming He willed from it and He willed for it, it is said: He willed all beings in their entirety, and it is not said: He willed from them and He willed for them. Rather, He willed them as they are, of renewal and specification by existence rather than non-existence.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage clarifies the terminology of divine will (irāda). It begins by explaining that the words 'displeasure' (sakhaṭ) and 'pleasure' (riḍā) are not anthropomorphic attributes but refer to God's blame or praise in this life and His reward or punishment in the afterlife. The author then cites the verse "Allah wishes to turn to you" and other similar verses, invoking a saying of Ja'far al-Ṣādiq to explain two kinds of divine will: what God wills for us (i.e., His commands) and what He wills from us (i.e., His preknowledge). The essence is that divine will is one but receives different names based on its object. When attached to reward, it is called riḍā (pleasure) and maḥabbah (love); when attached to punishment, it is sakhaṭ (displeasure) and ghaḍab (wrath). Further classifications are given: when attached to an object in the manner of knowledge (i.e., God knows what will happen from the object), it is said 'He willed from it what He knew'; in the manner of command, 'He willed for it what He commanded.' Finally, when will is directed to creation absolutely, with specification and determination, independent of human acquisition, it is simply 'He willed all beings.' The author emphasizes that God wills each being with its particular properties—renewal and specification to existence rather than non-existence—without saying 'He willed from them' or 'He willed for them' in that absolute sense.

رضا
سخط
محبة
غضب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure and anger in God imply emotional change or passion.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these terms refer to God's will in relation to reward and punishment, not to emotional states.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is divine will called ‘pleasure’ (rida)?
A) When it attaches to creation absolutely
B) When it attaches to reward
C) When it attaches to punishment
D) When it attaches to knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's pleasure and anger are one will named based on its object: reward or punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your sins, does the thought of God's anger frighten you as an unpredictable emotion, or as the certain consequence of His just will? How does today's lesson change which of these two views you hold—and what will you do about it tomorrow?
?
If God's pleasure is simply His will to reward you, not a changing mood, does that make His love feel more reliable or more distant? What does that imply about how you seek His pleasure in your daily choices?
?
Consider a moment you felt punished by life. Did you assume God was angry with you in an emotional sense? After this lesson, how might you reinterpret that trial as an expression of His singular will—and how should that affect your patience and repentance?

The Different Attachments of Divine Will
Classifies the attachments of divine will into three types: knowledge, command, and absolute creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the one divine will is described differently when it relates to knowledge, command, or absolute creation.

Core Idea
The same will takes different descriptions depending on its relationship to the object.

After establishing that divine will is one but named pleasure or anger based on reward/punishment, the author now distinguishes three further ways the will attaches: in relation to knowledge, command, and absolute creation. This prevents confusion about how God's will relates to human actions and existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three attachments
The passage explains three cases: when will attaches to an object in the manner of knowledge, in the manner of command, and absolutely to creation.
2
Understand the distinction
Note the different phrases: 'willed from it' (knowledge), 'willed for it' (command), and 'willed it' (absolute creation).
These are not different wills but different aspects.
3
Reflect on the absolute will
The final case is the most general: God wills all beings exactly as they are, with their specific properties, without using 'from' or 'for'.
This shows God's will encompasses everything.

Divine Will in Human Acts: Two Aspects
Sanusi presents two theories on divine will and human acts with scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways divine will relates to human acts—by command and by knowledge—and how Quranic verses support this distinction.

Core Idea
Divine will attaches to human acts both as command and as knowledge.

This passage clarifies a key theological question: how does Allah's will relate to human actions? The author presents two possibilities, then provides evidence from the Quran. Understanding these distinctions helps resolve apparent tensions between predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Get a sense of the overall argument.
Notice the 'either...or' structure at the beginning.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two views on how divine will attaches to human acts. Find the first position (only as creation) and the second (both as command and knowledge).
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Study the two or three Quranic verses cited. How does each support the second position? Think about the meanings of 'willing for us' and 'willing from us'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أفعال العباد من حيث إنها أفعالهم ؛ إما أن يقال : تتعلق الإرادة بها إلا على الوجه الذي ينسب إلى العباد بل على الوجه الذي انتسب إلى الخلق إيجادا وتخصيصاً، وإما أن يقال : تعلقت الإرادة بها على الوجهين المذكورين ؛ أنه أراد بنا وأراد منا أراد بنا ما أمر دينا وشرعا واعتقادًا ومذهبًا وأراد منا ما علم سابقة وعاقبة وفاتحة وخاتمة، ودل على صحة هذا المعنى قوله : ( وَلَوْ شِعْنَا لَأَتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَنهَا وَلَكِنْ حَقِّ الْقَوْلُ مِنِي لَأَمْلَأَنْ جَهَنَّمَ مِنَ الْجِنَّةِ وَالنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ ) [السجدة : ۱۳] ؛ أي لما لم يشأ الهداية لحق القول على مقتضى العلم السابق. وقوله تعالى : ( وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ) أي : لأمرهم بالعبادة، وقيل : ليخضعوا ويستسلموا كما قال : ( وَلَهُ أَسْلَمَ مَن في السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ طَوْعًا وَكَرْها ) ( ال عمران : ۱۸۳.
Then human acts, insofar as they are their acts: either it is said that the divine will attaches to them only in the aspect that is attributed to the servants, but rather in the aspect that is attributed to creation in terms of bringing into existence and specification; or it is said that the divine will attaches to them in the two mentioned aspects: that He willed for us and He willed from us—He willed for us what He commanded in religion, law, belief, and doctrine—and He willed from us what He knew in antecedent and consequent, beginning and end. And the evidence for the soundness of this meaning is His saying: "And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me has come true: I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people all together" (Sajda 32:13); that is, since He did not will guidance, the word came true according to previous knowledge. And His saying, exalted: "And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me" (Dhariyat 51:56); that is, to command them to worship; and it is said: to submit and surrender, as He said: "And to Him submits whoever is in the heavens and the earth, willingly or unwillingly" (Al Imran 3:83).
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses how divine will applies to human acts. The author presents two possible positions. First, divine will attaches to human acts only in the aspect that these acts are attributed to God's creation (bringing into existence and specification), not in the aspect they are attributed to the servants. Alternatively, divine will attaches in both aspects: what God wills for us (His commands in religion, legislation, belief, and doctrine) and what God wills from us (His knowledge of the beginning and end of each person). The author then cites two Quranic verses as evidence. From Surah Sajda (32:13): if God had willed to guide everyone, He would have, but His word (to fill Hell) must come true because His previous knowledge dictated that some would not be guided. The second verse (51:56) states that God created jinn and humans to worship Him, which is interpreted as commanding them to worship, or to submit, as the third verse (3:83) indicates that all beings submit to God willingly or unwillingly.

Key Terms
أفعال العباد
إرادة
إيجاد
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine will for human acts negates free will or that human actions are not truly willed by Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that divine will attaches to human acts in two aspects: command (what we should do) and knowledge (what will happen). This preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Checkpoint

A) Will for creation and will for command
B) Will for reward and will for punishment
C) Will for us (command) and will from us (knowledge)
D) Will as pleasure and will as anger

Key Takeaway
Allah's will relates to human acts both as what He commands (legislation) and as what He knows (destiny), reconciling divine decree with human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What one area of obedience have you neglected because you thought it was merely a suggestion, not a divine will?
?

?
If both command and knowledge are aspects of Allah's will for your acts, then neither justifies rebellion nor despair. Which of these two truths do you need to embrace more deeply right now, and what will you change today to live it?
