The Necessity of Some Evil for Universal Good
Classification of good and evil and argument for necessary evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some evil must exist in the world according to the author, and how it fits into the classification of good and evil.

Core Idea
Some evil is necessary to preserve the universal good.

This lesson presents a theological argument for why evil exists. The author classifies all possible things into absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed. He then argues that absolute evil does not exist, and that the partially evil things that exist are necessary for the greater good. This reasoning defends the wisdom of God in a world that contains evil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and list the three categories of things: absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed good and evil. Notice the subdivision of mixed into equal or predominant.
This classification is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand existence status
Note which categories actually exist: absolute good exists; absolute evil and equal/predominant evil have no existence. The only existing category is predominantly good mixed with some evil.
This sets up the necessity of some evil.
3
Grasp the necessity argument
The author argues that preventing a particular evil would lead to a greater evil or loss of universal good, so it is better for the mixed good to exist. The example of fire illustrates that its constant benefits outweigh occasional harms.
Focus on the logical 'greater evil' reasoning.
4
Reflect on the fire example
Fire is necessary for the universe but inevitably causes some harm. The permanent good from fire outweighs the accidental evil. This shows why some evil is tolerated.
This concretizes the abstract argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانقسمت الأمور إذا توهمت موجودة إلى خير مطلق، وإلى شر مطلق، وإلى خير ممزوج بشر، والأخير إما أن يتساوى فيه الخير والشر، أو يغلب أحدهما. أما الخير المطلق الذي لا شر فيه فقد وجد في الطباع وفي الخلقة، وأما الشر المطلق الذي لا خير فيه، والغالب أو المساوي فلا وجود له أصلا، فبقي ما الغالب في وجوده الخير وليس يخلو عن شر، فالأحرى به أن يوجد فإن لا يكون أعظم شرا من كونه، فواجب أن يقتضي وجوده من حيث يقتضي منه الوجود لئلا يفوت الخير الكلي بوجود الشر الجزئي، وأيضًا لو امتنع وجود ذلك القدر من الشر امتنع وجود أسبابه التي تؤدي إليه بالعرض، فكان منه أعظم حال في نظام الخير الكلي كمثل النار فإن الكون إنما يتم بأن تكون فيه نار، ولن يتصور وجودها إلا على وجه تحرق وتسخن، ولم يكن بد من المصادفات الحادثة أن تصادف النار ثوب فقير ناسك فتحرقه، فالأمر الدائم والأكثر حصول الخير من النار، أما الدائم
And things, if they are imagined to exist, are divided into absolute good, absolute evil, and good mixed with evil. And the last either has good and evil equal, or one of them predominates. As for absolute good that has no evil in it, it has been found in natures and in creation. As for absolute evil that has no good in it, and the predominant or equal, they have no existence at all. So what remains is that whose existence is predominantly good and is not free from evil. So it is more proper that it should exist, for if not, it would be a greater evil than its being. So it is necessary that its existence be necessitated from the aspect from which existence is necessitated from it, so that the universal good should not be lost due to the existence of particular evil. And also, if that amount of evil were prevented, its causes that lead to it accidentally would be prevented, so from it would be a greater state/damage in the system of universal good. For example, fire: the universe is only completed by having fire in it, and its existence cannot be conceived except in a manner that it burns and heats. And there is no escape from accidental occurrences that fire meets the garment of a poor ascetic and burns it. So the permanent and more frequent obtaining of good from fire, as for the permanent
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of entities into absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed good/evil. The mixed type may have equal or predominant good/evil. The author states that absolute good exists in natures and creation, but absolute evil, and the predominant or equal evil, have no existence. What remains is the predominantly good that is not free from evil. It is better for such a thing to exist, because preventing it would be a greater evil. The existence of some evil is necessary for the universal good (al-khayr al-kullī). If that particular evil were prevented, its accidental causes would be prevented, resulting in a greater disturbance in the system of universal good. The example of fire illustrates this: fire is necessary for the universe, but it inevitably burns some things. The benefit of fire is permanent and more frequent, while the harm is accidental and occasional.

Key Terms

الشر المطلق
الخير الكلي
الشر الجزئي
النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil contradicts God's goodness, so God must be unable to prevent it.
Correction: The author argues that some evil is necessary for the universal good, and preventing it would cause a greater evil. Therefore, evil does not contradict God's goodness but is part of the best possible order.

Checkpoint
What is the author's justification for the existence of some evil in the world?
A) Evil is an illusion, so it does not really exist.
B) Some evil is necessary to avoid a greater evil and to preserve universal good.
C) Evil is a punishment for sin alone.
D) God is unable to prevent evil.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that some evil is necessary to preserve universal good, and preventing it would lead to a greater evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a tragedy or deep suffering in the world, do you immediately question whether God truly cares, or do you pause to consider that this evil might be a necessary part of a greater good you cannot see? How can you train your heart to trust the universal good more than your immediate feelings?
?
Think of a personal loss or disappointment that felt entirely negative at the time. Can you now see any hidden good that came from it, or can you humbly accept that it may have served a purpose beyond your understanding? Does this lesson challenge you to reevaluate that hardship?
?
If you were given the power to remove every instance of evil from your life and from the world, would you do it without considering the consequences? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of your own judgment in defining what is truly good or harmful?

Essential and Accidental Will in Divine Decree
Classification of good and evil and distinction of divine will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni theologians distinguish God's essential will for good from His accidental will for evil, and why absolute evil does not exist in creation.

Core Idea
God wills good directly, evil only indirectly, and absolute evil has no real existence.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam lays the foundation for a theodicy that preserves God's unity and goodness. The author first clarifies that evil enters divine decree only accidentally. He then classifies things into absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed good-and-evil, arguing that absolute evil is nonexistent. This sets the stage for understanding why the world contains evil without attributing evil directly to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening line carefully
The opening line states that evil enters divine decree accidentally, not essentially. This is the key distinction.
Notice the terms 'bi al-dhāt' and 'bi al-ʻaraḍ'.
2
Identify the two types of divine will
God wills good essentially (irada bi al-dhāt) and wills evil accidentally (irada bi al-ʻaraḍ). He is the source of goods, not evils.
3
Understand the threefold classification
Things divide into absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed good-and-evil. The mixed kind may have equal or predominant parts.
4
Note that absolute evil does not exist
Absolute good exists in natures and creation. Absolute evil, predominant evil, or equal evil have no existence at all. Only predominantly good mixed things exist.
This is a metaphysical claim.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
This distinction preserves that God is the source of all good, and evil is only accidental. It avoids attributing evil to God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يهودانه وينصرانه ويمجسانه، فالشر إذا داخل في القضاء الإلهي بالعرض لا بالذات إذا وقع فبالحري أن يجازي مجازاة الهلاك والفساد على وجود العلة العارضة، فالباري تعالى مريد للخير إرادة بالذات؛ إذ هو مصدر للخيرات، ومريد للشر إرادة بالعرض وليس مصدر الشرور وانقسمت الأمور إذا توهمت موجودة إلى خير مطلق، وإلى شر مطلق، وإلى خير ممزوج بشر، والأخير إما أن يتساوى فيه الخير والشر، أو يغلب أحدهما. أما الخير المطلق الذي لا شر فيه فقد وجد في الطباع وفي الخلقة، وأما الشر المطلق الذي لا خير فيه، والغالب أو المساوي فلا وجود له أصلا، فبقي ما الغالب في وجوده الخير وليس يخلو عن شر، فالأحرى به أن يوجد فإن لا يكون أعظم شرا من كونه، فواجب أن يقتضي وجوده من حيث يقتضي منه الوجود لئلا يفوت الخير الكلي بوجود الشر الجزئي، وأيضا لو امتنع وجود ذلك القدر من الشر امتنع وجود أسبابه التي تؤدي إليه بالعرض، فكان منه أعظم حال في نظام الخير الكلي كمثل النار فإن الكون إنما يتم بأن تكون فيه نار، ولن يتصور وجودها إلا على وجه تحرق وتسخن، ولم يكن بد من المصادفات الحادثة أن تصادف النار ثوب فقير ناسك فتحرقه، فالأمر الدائم والأكثر حصول الخير من النار، أما الدائم فلأن أنواعا كثيرة لا تستحفظ على الدوام إلا بالنار، وأما الأكثر فإن أكثر أنواع الأشخاص في كنف السلامة عن الاحتراق، فما كان يحسن أن يترك المنافع الدائمة الأكثرية لأعراض شرية أقلية فإرادات الباري تعالى الخيرات الكائنة على مثال هذه الأشياء إرادة أولية على الوجه الذي يصلح أن يقال: أرادها بالذات وبالقصد الأول، وأراد الشرور الكائنة غير أولية على الوجه الذي يصلح أن يقال: أرادها إرادة بالعرض وبالقصد الثاني، فالخير مقتضى بالذات والشر مقتضى بالعرض، وكل يقدر ولو لم يقرر الأمر على ما قررناه للزم أن يكون مصدر الخير غير مصدر الشر، وذاك مذهب الثنوية.
They Judaize and Christianize and Magianize [it]. So evil, when it enters into divine decree accidentally (bi al-ʻaraḍ) not essentially (bi al-dhāt), when it occurs, then it is fitting that it be requited with requital of destruction and corruption upon the existence of the accidental cause. So the Most High Creator wills good by essential will, for He is the source of goods, and wills evil by accidental will, and He is not the source of evils. And things, if imagined to exist, divide into absolute good, absolute evil, and good mixed with evil. The latter, either good and evil are equal in it, or one of them predominates. As for absolute good in which there is no evil, it exists in natures and in creation. As for absolute evil in which there is no good, and that which is predominant or equal, it has no existence at all. So what remains is that whose existence is predominantly good and is not free from evil. It is more appropriate that it should exist, for if not, its non-existence would be a greater evil than its existence. So it is necessary that its existence be necessitated from the aspect from which existence is necessitated from it, so that the universal good not be lost due to the existence of the particular evil. Also, if that amount of evil were prevented, the causes that lead to it accidentally would be prevented, and there would be a greater disruption in the system of universal good. Like the example of fire: the cosmos is only perfected by having fire in it, and its existence cannot be conceived except in a way that burns and heats, and it was unavoidable that accidental occurrences would occur such that fire meets the garment of a poor ascetic and burns it. So the perpetual and more frequent occurrence of good from fire: as for the perpetual, because many types cannot be preserved perpetually except by fire; as for the more frequent, because most types of individuals are in the shelter of safety from burning. So it would not be good to abandon the perpetual and more frequent benefits for occasional evil accidents. So the willings of the Most High of the goods occurring on the pattern of these things are primary willings, in the manner that it is proper to say: He willed them essentially and by first intention, and He willed the occurring evils non-primarily, in the manner that it is proper to say: He willed them by accidental will and by second intention. So good is necessitated essentially, and evil is necessitated accidentally. And everything is decreed. And if the matter had not been settled as we have settled it, it would necessitate that the source of good is other than the source of evil, and that is the doctrine of the dualists (al-thunawiyya).
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the relationship of evil to divine decree. It begins by stating that evil enters divine decree accidentally, not essentially. God is the source of goods and wills good essentially, but wills evil only accidentally. Then it classifies things into absolute good, absolute evil, and mixed good-and-evil. Absolute good exists in creation; absolute evil does not exist at all. What exists is predominantly good but mixed with some evil. The author argues that such mixed things should exist because the good outweighs the evil; preventing the partial evil would lead to greater harm to universal good. The example of fire illustrates this: fire is necessary for the cosmos but can cause accidental harm. God wills good primarily and evil secondarily. If one denied this, one would fall into dualism, positing two separate sources of good and evil. The argument is both a classification and a proof for divine unity and wisdom.

بالعرض
الخير المطلق
الشر المطلق
الخير الكلي
الشر الجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that evil must be directly willed by God because it exists.
Correction: The passage clarifies that evil enters divine decree accidentally, not essentially. God wills only good essentially.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does God will evil?
A) Essentially
B) Accidentally
C) Not at all
D) Only indirectly through secondary causes

Key Takeaway
God wills good essentially and evil accidentally; absolute evil does not exist; only predominantly good mixed things exist in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a recent hardship, how easy is it for you to see it as accidentally willed by God rather than as something He intends directly? What would change in your reaction if you truly believed that God wills only good essentially and that evil is accidental?
?
The passage says absolute evil does not exist. Think of a person or situation you consider purely evil. How does this teaching challenge that judgment? Can you find any trace of good in that person or situation? If not, what does that say about your own perspective?
?
If every existing thing contains predominantly good and some evil, how does that affect your gratitude to God? Are you more inclined to focus on the good or the evil? Commit to one specific way you will practice seeing the predominant good in a difficult relationship this week.

The Principle of Predominant Good and Refutation of Dualism
Argument from predominant good and refutation of dualism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God wills mixed goods for the sake of the overall good, illustrated by the example of fire, and that this refutes dualism.

Core Idea
God permits partial evil to achieve universal good, and any other view leads to dualism.

In this continuation, the author defends the existence of the mixed good—things that are predominantly beneficial but entail some harm. Using the example of fire, he shows that the good of fire outweighs its occasional harm. Thus, God wills such things primarily, and the evil they contain only secondarily. Denying this would imply two separate sources of good and evil, the doctrine of the dualists.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for existence of mixed good
The passage argues that mixed good should exist because its non-existence would be a greater evil. This is a utilitarian principle.
2
Study the fire example carefully
Fire is necessary for the cosmos but can burn things. The perpetual and frequent good of fire outweighs occasional harm.
This illustrates the principle.
3
Understand primary and secondary will
God wills goods primarily (by first intention) and evils secondarily (by second intention). This ties back to the essential/accidental distinction.
4
See how the argument refutes dualism
If one denies that evil is accidental, one must posit two sources of good and evil, which is the dualist heresy.
5
Summarize the overall theodicy
The passage provides a coherent theodicy: God wills good, permits evil for greater good, and is the sole source of existence.

God Wills Good Primarily, Evil Secondarily
Al-Shahrastani argues that God wills good primarily and evil secondarily to avoid dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani argues that God wills good primarily and evil secondarily, and why this distinction is necessary to avoid dualism.

Core Idea
Good is willed essentially; evil only accidentally.

This passage tackles a deep theological problem: if God is all-good, how can He will evil? Al-Shahrastani argues that God wills good primarily and evil only secondarily and accidentally. This distinction preserves divine unity by avoiding the dualist belief that good and evil come from separate sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow from the fire analogy to the conclusion about divine will.
The fire analogy is key to understanding the argument.
2
Identify the two types of will
Find where the passage distinguishes between God's primary/essential will (for good) and secondary/accidental will (for evil). Understand the terms 'first intention' and 'second intention'.
These terms are crucial for the argument.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the alternative: if this distinction were not made, it would imply that good and evil come from different sources—the belief of the Dualists. Ask yourself why this is problematic for Islamic theology.
The passage ends by warning against dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلأن أنواعاً كثيرة لا تستحفظ على الدوام إلا بالنار، وأما الأكثر فإن أكثر أنواع الأشخاص في كنف السلامة عن الاحتراق، فما كان يحسن أن يترك المنافع الدائمة الأكثرية لأعراض شرية أقلية، فإرادات الباري تعالى الخيرات الكائنة على مثال هذه الأشياء إرادة أولية على الوجه الذي يصلح أن يقال: أرادها بالذات وبالقصد الأول، وأراد الشرور الكائنة غير أولية على الوجه الذي يصلح أن يقال: أرادها إرادة بالعرض وبالقصد الثاني، فالخير مقتضى بالذات والشر مقتضى بالعرض، ولو لم يقرر الأمر على ما قررناه للزم أن يكون مصدر الخير غير مصدر الشر، وذاك مذهب الثنوية.
For many species are preserved only through fire, while most individuals are safe from burning, so it would not be fitting to abandon the permanent majority benefits for minority evil accidents. Thus, the will of God Most High for the goods that occur on the pattern of these things is a primary will, in the sense that it may be said: He willed them essentially and with first intention; and He willed the occurring evils non-primarily, in the sense that it may be said: He willed them accidentally and with second intention. So good is required essentially and evil is required accidentally. And if the matter were not settled as we have settled it, it would necessarily follow that the source of good is other than the source of evil, and that is the doctrine of the Dualists.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the divine will regarding good and evil. It begins by noting that many species are preserved by fire, while most individuals are safe from burning; thus it is not wise to abandon permanent majority benefits for minority evil accidents. The author then distinguishes two types of divine will: God wills good things primarily and essentially (with first intention), and wills evils non-primarily and accidentally (with second intention). Therefore, good is required essentially, evil accidentally. If this distinction were not made, it would force the conclusion that good and evil come from different sources—the belief of the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya). The passage thus defends divine unity by maintaining that God wills everything, but good and evil are willed in different modes.

Key Terms
الخير
الشر
الإرادة
القصد الأول
القصد الثاني
مقتضى بالذات
مقتضى بالعرض
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God wills evil in the same way He wills good.
Correction: God wills evil only accidentally and with second intention, not essentially.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does God will evil?
A) Essentially and with first intention
B) Accidentally and with second intention
C) Not at all
D) Only through human actions

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani distinguishes primary and secondary divine will to affirm that God wills good essentially while evil is willed only accidentally, thereby upholding divine unity against dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world—whether your own mistakes or injustice around you—do you genuinely believe that God wills it only accidentally, as a means to a greater good? Or do you sometimes feel He has turned away from His goodness? How would your response to suffering change if you fully internalized that His primary will is only good?
?
You often ask 'Why did this bad thing happen to me?'—but this lesson asks you to rephrase: 'What greater good might God be intentionally bringing about, such that this evil is a necessary shadow?' Are you willing to search for that good, trusting that God's first intention is always good, even when you cannot see it? Or do you let the evil define your view of His will?
?
Al-Shahrastani warns that rejecting this distinction leads to dualism—believing that good and evil come from separate sources. In your own heart, when you experience intense hardship, do you ever feel as if there is a dark force opposing God? This lesson calls you to reject that dualism and to affirm that the very same God who is the source of all good also permits evil with a secondary will. Can you surrender to that truth without resentment, and thank Him even in trials?

The Problem of Necessary Emanation and the Definition of Good
Al-Shahrastani critiquing necessary emanation and the definition of good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Shahrastani argues that the opponent's system of necessary emanation collapses the distinction between essential and accidental will, and why defining good as that which all desire makes God the desired, not the desirer.

Core Idea
Necessary emanation makes everything equally willed, undermining the very concept of will.

Al-Shahrastani begins his refutation by imposing a universal argument: if everything emanates necessarily from the First, then nothing is essentially willed versus accidentally willed. He then attacks the opponent's definition of good, showing it makes God the object of desire, not the subject. This lesson unpacks these two critical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the logical structure: first a universal claim, then a critique of the definition of good.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the universal matter
The author says that if all existence emanates as necessary concomitants, then everything is more like a second intention than a first intention. Ask yourself: what is the difference between 'essential' and 'accidental' will in this system?
‘Second intention’ means secondary, not primary.
3
Analyze the critique of the definition of good
The opponent defines good as that which all desire. The author responds that the Creator does not desire good; He is desired by all. Therefore, He is the desired, not the desirer, which contradicts the opponent's own principle of will.
This exposes an inconsistency.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Notice how the author uses these points to show that the opponent's system cannot sustain a coherent theory of divine will. This sets up further critiques in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا نلزمكم أمرًا كليا فإن عندكم الوجود كله بما فيه من الروحاني والجسماني صادر عن الأول على الترتيب المذكور صدور اللوازم عن الشيء، وما كان على سبيل اللوازم فهو بالقصد الثاني أشبه منه بالقصد الأول، فما الفرق بين الشرور الواقعة في الكائنات وكونها مقتضية بالعرض وبين أصل الكون والكائنات وحصولها على سبيل اللزوم؟ فليس إذا في الوجود شيء هو مقتضى بالذات حتى يكون شيء آخر هو مقتضى بالعرض، وما ذكرتموه من حد الخير أنه يتشوقه الكل يبطل قاعدتكم في الإرادة فإن الباري لا يتشوق الخير، بل يتشوقه الكل فهو الخير المحض وهو المراد لا المريد، وما سواه فليس بخير ولا مراد؛ إذ لا يتشوقه الباري تعالى فلم يرده ويرجع الكلام إلى شبه الطامات. وقولكم : إنه إذا علم النظام وأبدعه فقد أراده فجعلتم الإرادة مركبة من سلب وإضافة، أما السلب فمن جهة أنه عالم؛ أي غير محتجب عن ذاته بذاته، وأما الإضافة فمن جهة أنه مبدأ النظام الخير، والنظام في الوجود تابع ولازم لكونه عالما، والشر في الوجود لازم وتبع للنظام، فما الفرق بين لازم ولازم؟ وعندكم الجزئيات معلومة تتبع الكليات والكليات معلومة تبعا لعلمه بذاته، وهي معلومة على منهاج كونها معلومة، فالشر والقبائح يجب أن تكون مرادة على
But we impose upon you a universal matter: for according to you, all existence—including the spiritual and the corporeal—emanates from the First in the mentioned order as the emanation of necessary concomitants from a thing. And whatever is on the mode of necessary concomitants is more akin to second intention than to first intention. So what difference is there between the evils occurring in the universe and their being required accidentally, and between the origin of the universe and its occurrences and their coming to be on the mode of necessity? Thus there is nothing in existence that is required essentially such that something else is required accidentally. And what you mentioned concerning the definition of good—that it is desired by all—invalidates your principle concerning will, for the Creator does not desire good; rather, it is desired by all, so He is pure good and He is the desired, not the desirer. And other than Him is neither good nor desired, since the Creator does not desire it, so He did not will it, and the discussion returns to resembling absurdities. And your statement that when He knows the order and originated it, He willed it—thus you made will composed of negation and relation: as for negation, because He is knowing, i.e., not veiled from His essence by His essence; and as for relation, because He is the principle of the good order. And the order in existence is a consequence and necessary concomitant of His being knowing, and evil in existence is a necessary concomitant and consequence of order. So what difference is there between a necessary concomitant and a necessary concomitant? And according to you, particulars are known following universals, and universals are known as a consequence of His knowledge of His essence, and they are known according to the mode of their being known. So evil and ugly things must be willed...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's metaphysical system. The author begins by imposing a universal argument: according to the opponent, all existence emanates necessarily from the First Principle as necessary concomitants (lawazim). Things that are necessary concomitants are more akin to second intention than first intention. Therefore, the author asks, what difference can there be between evils occurring as accidental requirements and the entire universe existing by necessity? If everything is necessary, nothing is essentially willed as opposed to accidentally willed. The author then attacks the opponent's definition of good as 'that which all desire.' Since the Creator does not desire (tashawwuq) but is desired by all, He would be the desired, not the desirer—contradicting their own theory of divine will. Next, the author criticizes the opponent's claim that knowledge of the order implies volition: they make will a compound of negation (God is knowing, i.e., not ignorant) and relation (He is the source of good order). But if order follows necessarily from knowledge, and evil follows necessarily from order, then both are equally necessary concomitants. The passage concludes with an argument that since particulars are known as following from universals, and universals are known from God's self-knowledge, then even evil and ugly things must be willed (the sentence is incomplete, but the implication is that the opponent cannot avoid attributing evil to divine will).

Key Terms
اللوازم
القصد الأول
القصد الثاني
التشوق
الخير المحض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Al-Shahrastani is accepting the emanationist framework and merely adjusting it.
Correction: In fact, he is showing that the framework leads to logical contradictions, especially regarding divine will and the definition of good.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's argument against the opponent's definition of good?
A) Good is what all desire, so God must desire it too
B) God is pure good and desired, not desirer, so the definition fails for God
C) Good and evil are both necessary
D) The opponent has no definition of good

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani's universal argument shows that necessary emanation eliminates any meaningful distinction between essential and accidental will, and his critique of the definition of good reveals that it makes God the desired, not the desirer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe God's goodness means He desires good for you, does this lesson challenge you to reexamine whether you see God as the object of your desire or the subject who wills? How does your worship reflect that?
?
Al-Shahrastani argues that in necessary emanation, there is no real distinction between good and evil—everything is necessary. Do you sometimes live as if your sins are 'necessary' outcomes rather than choices you made? What does that reveal about your view of God's will?
?
The definition of good as 'what all desire' makes God the desired, not the desirer. Are you living as if God is the one who must desire your good actions, or are you desiring Him above all? What needs to change?

The Compound Will and the Problem of Evil
Al-Shahrastani deconstructs the opponent's theory of will and deduces evil is necessarily willed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Shahrastani deconstructs the opponent's theory of will as a compound of negation and relation, and why this leads to the conclusion that evil must be willed as a necessary concomitant of order.

Core Idea
If will is merely knowledge and relation, then evil is as necessary as good.

Al-Shahrastani now turns to the opponent's specific account of divine will. The opponent claims that God's will arises from His knowledge of the good order: when He knows the order, He wills it. The author argues that this makes will a mere compound of negation (God is not ignorant) and relation (He is the source of order). Consequently, evil, being a necessary concomitant of order, becomes equally willed. This lesson examines this crucial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the structure: the opponent's claim, the author's analysis of will as compound, and the problem of evil.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument is clear enough.
2
Identify the opponent's position
The opponent says: when God knows the order, He originates it, and this is His will. The author breaks this down into two components: negation (God is not veiled from His essence) and relation (He is the principle of good order).
‘Salb’ means negation; ‘idafa’ means relation.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If order follows necessarily from knowledge, and evil follows necessarily from order, then both are necessary concomitants. There is no room for choice or distinction.
The author asks: 'What difference between one necessary concomitant and another?'
4
Extend to particulars
Since particulars follow from universals, and universals follow from God's self-knowledge, even evil and ugly things must be willed. The opponent cannot escape this conclusion.
The passage is incomplete but the implication is clear.

Refutation of the Definition of Good as Universal Desire
Refutation of the definition of good as universal desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the definition of good as 'what all desire' by showing that it contradicts the nature of divine will.

Core Idea
If good is what all desire, then God is the desired, not the desirer.

The author now turns to refute a specific definition of good proposed by opponents: that good is whatever all beings desire. This definition might seem intuitive, but it conflicts with the Islamic understanding of God's will and perfection. In this short but dense passage, the author demonstrates that this definition leads to absurd consequences and undermines the opponent's own principle of will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's definition
The passage begins by quoting the opponent's definition of good as 'that which all desire'. Recognise this as the starting point of the argument.
2
Apply the definition to God
Notice the author's key move: if good is what all desire, then God cannot be said to desire good in the same sense; rather, all desire God. This flips the roles.
3
Draw the consequences
See how the author concludes that God is the pure good and the desired, not the desirer, and that anything other than God is neither good nor desired by Him. Understand why this makes the opponent's definition self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكرتموه من حد الخير أنه يتشوقه الكل يبطل قاعدتكم في الإرادة فإن الباري لا يتشوق الخير، بل يتشوقه الكل فهو الخير المحض وهو المراد لا المريد، وما سواه فليس بخير ولا مراد؛ إذ لا يتشوقه الباري تعالى فلم يرده ويرجع الكلام إلى شبه الطامات.
And what you have mentioned from the definition of good that it is that which all desire invalidates your principle concerning will: for the Creator does not desire good, rather all desire it, so He is pure good and He is the desired, not the one who desires; and whatever is other than Him is neither good nor desired, since the Creator, exalted, does not desire it, so He did not will it. And the discourse returns to resembling absurdities.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's definition of good (خير) as 'that which all desire'. The author shows that this definition undermines the opponent's own concept of divine will. If good is defined by being desired by all, then God (the Creator) does not desire good in that sense—rather, all created things desire Him as the ultimate good. Thus God is the object of desire (المراد), not the one who desires (المريد). Anything other than God is neither good nor desired, because God does not desire it. This leads to absurd conclusions, as the opponent's principle of will fails.

تشوق (desire)
إرادة (will)
باري (Creator)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying that God is good.
Correction: The author is not denying God's goodness; he is showing that the opponent's definition of good as 'what all desire' leads to God being the desired, not the desirer, which contradicts the opponent's own view on divine will.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God is the desired, not the desirer, what does that mean for your worship? Is your relationship with God about seeking Him as the ultimate good, or about using Him to get what you desire? Which is true in your life right now, and what needs to change?
?
When you say 'God wills good for me,' are you using a human concept of good that might be completely wrong? This passage shows that God's goodness is not about Him desiring something outside Himself. Could it be that what you call 'good' is not what God calls good? How will you adjust your expectations of God's will based on this?

Critique of the Opponent's Composite Concept of Will
Author analyzes opponent's view of divine will as composite of negation and relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues against the opponent's definition of divine will as a composite of negation and relation, and why this leads to the conclusion that evil must be willed.

Core Idea
Reducing will to knowledge and order makes evil equally willed.

In this passage, the author presents the opponent's view of divine will as a combination of negation (God's knowledge) and relation (being the source of order). He then shows that this leads to a problem: if evil follows necessarily from order as a concomitant, then it must also be willed. He asks a pointed question to highlight the inconsistency, setting up his own counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's use of 'your statement' which directly addresses the opponent.
2
Identify the two components of will
The author states that the opponent makes will composed of negation (absence of concealment) and relation (being the principle of good order). Find where these are described.
Negation is linked to God's omniscience, relation to the origin of order.
3
Trace the logical chain
Observe the sequence: knowledge → order as necessary concomitant → evil as necessary concomitant of order. The author then asks: what difference is there between these concomitants?
This question undermines any attempt to claim evil is not willed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that evil and abominable things must be willed in the same manner they are known. Consider why this is a problem for the opponent.
The opponent claims pure evil does not exist, but the author's analysis suggests otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم : إنه إذا علم النظام وأبدعه فقد أراده فجعلتم الإرادة مركبة من سلب وإضافة، أما السلب فمن جهة أنه عالم؛ أي غير محتجب عن ذاته بذاته، وأما الإضافة فمن جهة أنه مبدأ النظام الخير، والنظام في الوجود تابع ولازم لكونه عالما، والشر في الوجود لازم وتبع للنظام، فما الفرق بين لازم ولازم؟ وعندكم الجزئيات معلومة تتبع الكليات والكليات معلومة تبعا لعلمه، وهي معلومة على منهاج كونها معلومة، فالشر والقبائح يجب أن تكون مرادة على منهاج كونها معلومة، وقولكم الشر المحض ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الوجود اشتمل على الخير أو هو خير كله.
And your statement: When He knows the order and creates it, He has willed it. So you make will composed of negation and relation: as for the negation, it is from the aspect that He is knowing, i.e., not veiled from His essence by His essence; and as for the relation, it is from the aspect that He is the principle of the good order. And order in existence is a consequence and necessary concomitant of His being knowing, and evil in existence is a necessary concomitant and follower of order. So what difference is there between one necessary concomitant and another? And according to you, particulars are known and follow the universals, and universals are known as followers of His knowledge, and they are known in the manner of their being known. So evil and abominable things must be willed in the manner of their being known. And your statement that pure evil does not exist at all, for existence includes good or is entirely good.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the opponent's theory of divine will. The author explains that the opponent defines will as composed of two aspects: (1) a negation—that God's knowledge is not veiled from Himself, and (2) a relation—that God is the cause of the good order. The opponent holds that order follows necessarily from God's knowledge, and evil follows necessarily from that order. The author then asks: what difference is there between necessary consequences? If both good and evil are necessary concomitants, they are on the same footing. Furthermore, since all particulars are known and follow universals, and all knowledge is according to its manner, evil and abominable things must also be willed in the same way. The author then quotes the opponent's claim that pure evil does not exist, but existence either includes good or is entirely good. This sets up the next response.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
سلب (negation)
إضافة (relation)
لازم (necessary concomitant)
جزئيات (particulars)
كليات (universals)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is rejecting the existence of divine will entirely.
Correction: No, the author is critiquing the opponent's reductive definition, not will itself. He shows that if will equals knowledge and order, then evil must be willed, which the opponent denies.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the opponent's view of divine will?
A) Will is a separate attribute from knowledge.
B) Will is composed of negation and relation, based on knowledge and order.
C) Will is identical to creation.
D) Will is arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
By reducing will to knowledge and order, the opponent's view forces the conclusion that evil is equally necessary and thus must be willed, undermining their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this passage challenge you to accept that both good and evil are within His will without trying to explain it away?

God's Eternal Speech: Two Rational Proofs
Presentation of two methods proving God's eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods (Ash'ari and Isfarayini) used to prove that God speaks with eternal speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is proven through reason and the perfection of His actions.

This principle affirms that God is speaking with eternal speech, a belief held by the Islamic community. The author presents two distinct rational methods from Ash'ari theologians and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. We will explore the complete Ash'ari argument and the opening of al-Isfarayini's alternative approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the two methods introduced.
The passage is cut off at the end, so the second method is incomplete.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari method
Identify the key premises: reason shows God is living; living implies speech; otherwise deficiency; God is a king who commands; and the analogy with power/knowledge.
This method uses a 'from opposite' argument and analogy.
3
Understand al-Isfarayini's method
Notice that it begins with the perfection of actions proving God's knowledge, then argues that it is impossible for Him to know something without...
The argument is incomplete, but it shows a different starting point.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider why the author emphasizes that no Muslim disagrees, and why the proofs matter for affirming prophethood and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية عشرة في كون الباري متكلما بكلام أزلي. ولما لم نجد في الملة الإسلامية من يخالفنا في كون الباري تعالى متكلما بكلام قدمنا هذه المسألة وإن جرت العادة بتقديم المسألة الأخيرة، ولم يخالفنا في ذلك إلا الفلاسفة والصابية ومنكرو النبوات وطرق متكلمي الإسلام تختلف. فطريق الأشعرية أن قالوا: دل العقل على كون الباري تعالى حيا، والحي منه أن يتكلم ويأمر وينهي كما منه كما يصح أن يعلم ويقدر ويريد ويسمع ويبصر، فلو لم يتصف بالكلام أدى إلى أن يكون متصفا بضده وهو الخرس والعي والحصر وهي نقائص ويتعالى عنها. والذي يحقق هذه الطريقة قولهم: قد ثبت بدليل العقل أنه ملك مطاع ومن حكم الملك أن يكون منه أمر ونهي كما دل تردد الخلق في صنوف التغايير والحوادث والجائزات على كون الباري تعالى قادرًا عالما، دل تردد الخلق في صنوف الأمر والنهي على أمر الباري ونهيه وكما جرى في ملكه تقديره، جرى على عباده تكليفه، وكما تصرف في الموجودات الجبرية جبرا وقهرا تصرف في الموجودات الاختيارية تكليفا وتعريفا. وقد سلك الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني رحمه الله منهاجا آخر، فقال: دلت الأفعال بإتقانها وإحكامها على أنه تعالى عالم، ويستحيل أن يعلم شيئا
The twelfth principle: Concerning the Exalted Creator being speaking with eternal speech. Since we have not found in the Islamic community anyone who disagrees with us about the Exalted Creator being speaking with speech, we have preceded this issue even though the custom is to present the latter issue first. And none disagree with us in this except the philosophers, the Sabians, and the deniers of prophethoods. And the methods of the theologians of Islam differ. The way of the Ash'aris is that they said: Reason indicates that the Exalted Creator is living, and from the living it is that he speaks and commands and forbids, just as it is valid that he knows, wills, hears, and sees. So if he were not described with speech, it would lead to him being described with its opposite, which is muteness, speechlessness, and confinement, which are deficiencies, and He is exalted above them. And what verifies this method is their saying: It has been established by rational proof that He is a King obeyed, and it is a ruling of the king that from him come command and prohibition, just as the fluctuation of creation in kinds of changes, occurrences, and contingencies indicates that the Exalted Creator is powerful and knowing. So the fluctuation of creation in kinds of command and prohibition indicates His command and prohibition. And just as His decree runs in His kingdom, His obligation runs for His servants. And just as He disposed in necessary existents by compulsion and subjugation, He disposed in voluntary existents by obligation and definition. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, may God have mercy on him, followed another method. He said: Actions, by their perfection and precision, indicate that He is knowing, and it is impossible that He knows something
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is the twelfth principle, which establishes that God is speaking with eternal speech. The author notes that no one in the Islamic community disagrees, except the philosophers, Sabians, and deniers of prophethood. He then presents two theological methods. The Ash'ari method: reason indicates God is living; living implies speaking, commanding, forbidding, etc. If He lacks speech, He would have its opposite (muteness), which is a deficiency. Further, God is a King obeyed; a king issues commands and prohibitions. Just as the diversity of contingent things proves His power and knowledge, the diversity of commands and prohibitions proves His command and prohibition. The second method by al-Isfarayini: the perfection of actions proves God's knowledge, and it is impossible that He knows something... (the text cuts off).

Correction: The passage affirms that God speaks with eternal speech, and the Ash'ari method proves that speech is a necessary attribute of the living God, not a created accident.

Key Takeaway

The Ash'ari Proof from Life and Kingship
Ash'ari proof that God's life and kingship necessitate His eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that God's life implies speech and His kingship implies command and prohibition, establishing that He speaks with eternal speech.

Core Idea
A living king must speak—God's life and kingship necessitate His eternal speech.

The Ash'aris provide two intertwined arguments: first, reason shows God is living, and living beings speak; second, reason shows God is a king obeyed, and kings command. If God were mute, He would have a deficiency, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks these arguments from Sanusi's text.

Guided Path
1
Introduction to the principle
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states that only philosophers and deniers oppose the eternal speech of God. Notice that Ash'aris have a distinct method.
This sets the stage for the proof.
2
The argument from life
Examine how reason indicates God is living, and living beings naturally speak. If God lacked speech, He would have the defect of muteness.
This is the first of two arguments.
3
The argument from kingship
Consider the proof that God is a king obeyed; kings command and forbid. The variety of commands in creation points to God's command.
This reinforces the necessity of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية عشرة في كون الباري متكلما بكلام أزلي. ولما لم نجد في الملة الإسلامية من يخالفنا في كون الباري تعالى متكلما بكلام قدمنا هذه المسألة وإن جرت العادة بتقديم المسألة الأخيرة، ولم يخالفنا في ذلك إلا الفلاسفة والصابية ومنكرو النبوات وطرق متكلمي الإسلام تختلف. فطريق الأشعرية أن قالوا: دل العقل على كون الباري تعالى حيا، والحي منه أن يتكلم ويأمر وينهي كما يصح أن يعلم ويقدر ويريد ويسمع ويبصر، فلو لم يتصف بالكلام أدى إلى أن يكون متصفا بضده وهو الخرس والعي والحصر وهي نقائص ويتعالى عنها. والذي يحقق هذه الطريقة قولهم: قد ثبت بدليل العقل أنه ملك مطاع ومن حكم الملك أن يكون منه أمر ونهي. كما دل تردد الخلق في صنوف التغايير والحوادث والجائزات على كون الباري تعالى قادرا عالما، دل تردد الخلق في صنوف الأمر والنهي على أمر الباري ونهيه. وكما جرى في ملكه تقديره، جرى على عباده تكليفه، وكما تصرف في الموجودات الجبرية جبرا وقهرا تصرف في الموجودات الاختيارية تكليفا وتعريفا. وقد سلك الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني رحمه الله منهاجا آخر، فقال: دلت الأفعال بإتقانها وإحكامها على أنه تعالى عالم، ويستحيل أن يعلم شيئا ولا يخبر عنه فإن الخبر والعلم يتلازمان، فلا يتصور وجود أحدهما دون الثاني، ومن لا خبر عنده عن معلومه لا يمكنه أن يخبر غيره عنه، ومن المعلوم أن الباري يصح منه التكليف والتعريف والإخبار والتنبيه والإرشاد والتعليم، فوجب أن يكون له كلام وقول يكلف ويعرف ويخبر وينبه بذلك، فإذا ثبتت هذه الدلائل كونه متكلما. فنقول: إما أن يقال: هو متكلم لنفسه أو متكلم بكلام، ثم إن كان متكلما بكلام، فإما أن يكون كلامه قديما أو حادثا، وإن كان حادثا، فإما أن يحدث في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في محل، ولا قائل بكونه متكلما لنفسه من المعتزلة وغيرهم؛ إذ لا يعقل أزلا وأبدا، ولا قائل بكلام يخلقه لا في محل؛ لأن في نفي المحل نفي الاختصاص، وفيه إبطال التفرقة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يحدثه في ذاته كما صارت إليه الكرامية فقد سبق الرد عليهم، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل كما صار إليه المعتزلة فقد خالف قضية العقل؛ فإن الكلام لو قام بمحل لكان المحل متصفا به دون غيره من الفاعل وغيره كما لو تحرك متحرك بحركة يخلقها الله لم يرجع أخص وصفها إلى الفاعل، وكذلك سائر الأعراض، فبقي أنه متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي يختص به قياما ووصفا، وذلك ما أثبتناه.
The twelfth principle: Concerning that the Creator is a speaker with eternal speech. Since we have not found in the Islamic community anyone who disagrees with us that the Creator, exalted is He, is a speaker with speech, we have presented this issue even though the custom is to present the latter issue first, and only the philosophers, the Sabians, and the deniers of prophethood have disagreed with us in this, and the methods of the theologians of Islam differ. So the method of the Ash'aris is that they said: Reason indicates that the Creator, exalted is He, is living, and it is proper for the living to speak, command, and forbid, just as it is proper for him to know, have power, will, hear, and see. So if He were not described with speech, it would lead to His being described with its opposite, which is muteness, speechlessness, and incapacity, which are deficiencies, and He is exalted above them. That which verifies this method is their statement: It has been established by the evidence of reason that He is a king obeyed, and it is a rule of kingship that there be from him command and prohibition. Just as the oscillation of creation in the types of changes, events, and possibilities indicated that the Creator, exalted is He, is powerful and knowing, so the oscillation of creation in the types of command and prohibition indicates the command and prohibition of the Creator. And just as in His kingdom His decree runs, so upon His servants His obligation runs, and just as He disposes of necessary existents by compulsion and subjugation, He disposes of voluntary existents by obligation and definition. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, may God have mercy on him, followed another path, saying: Actions, by their perfection and precision, indicate that He, exalted is He, is knowing, and it is impossible that He knows something but does not inform about it, for informing and knowledge are inseparable, so it is inconceivable to have one without the other, and one who has no information about his known object cannot inform others about it. And it is known that obligation, definition, informing, alerting, guidance, and teaching are proper for the Creator, so it is necessary that He have speech and a word by which He obligates, defines, informs, and alerts. So when these proofs are established, He is a speaker. Then we say: Either it is said that He is a speaker in Himself or a speaker by speech. Then if He is a speaker by speech, either His speech is eternal or originated. And if it is originated, either it comes to be in His essence, or in a locus, or not in a locus. There is no proponent among the Mu'tazila and others that He is a speaker in Himself, for it is not intellectually conceivable eternally and ever. And there is no proponent of speech that He creates not in a locus, because negating the locus negates specification, and in it is the invalidation of the distinction between what subsists in itself and what does not subsist in itself. And whoever says that He is a speaker by speech that originates in His essence, as the Karramiyya held, the refutation against them has already preceded. And whoever says that He is a speaker by speech that He creates in a locus, as the Mu'tazila held, has contradicted the dictate of reason; for if speech were to subsist in a locus, the locus would be described with it to the exclusion of the agent and others, just as if a moving thing moves by a motion that God creates, its most specific description does not revert to the agent, and similarly for all accidents. So it remains that He is a speaker by eternal speech that He specifically owns in subsistence and description, and that is what we have established.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification aimed at establishing that God's speech is eternal. It begins by stating the topic: the twelfth principle is that God is a speaker with eternal speech. The author notes that only philosophers, Sabians, and deniers of prophethood dispute this. The Ash'ari method is then presented: reason shows God is living, and speech is a necessary concomitant of life; without it, God would have defects like muteness. This is reinforced by the 'king' argument: God is a king obeyed, and kingship implies command and prohibition; just as the variety in creation points to God's power and knowledge, the variety of commands and prohibitions points to His command and prohibition. Then Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini offers a supplementary proof: perfect actions indicate God's knowledge, and knowledge entails informing; since God can obligate, inform, etc., He must have speech. After these proofs, the author classifies possible positions: either God is a speaker in Himself (which is inconceivable) or by speech. If by speech, it is either eternal or originated. If originated, it either inheres in God's essence (Karramiyya, already refuted), in a locus (Mu'tazila, refuted because the locus would be described, not God), or nowhere (invalid because it negates specification). Thus, only the Ash'ari position remains: God is a speaker by eternal speech that subsists in His essence. The argument is structured as a disjunctive syllogism, eliminating all alternatives.

Key Terms
كلام
حي
ملك مطاع
أمر ونهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, what would it mean if God were not described with speech?
A) He would be ignorant.
B) He would be mute and deficient.
C) He would be incapable of commanding.
D) He would not be a king.

Key Takeaway
God's life and kingship necessitate that He has eternal speech; otherwise, He would have defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you approach it as a personal, urgent address from your Creator, or as a distant text? What will you change about your recitation or study this week?

Al-Isfarayini's Proof from Knowledge and Informing
Al-Isfarayini's proof from knowledge and the necessity of informing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Isfarayini's argument that God's perfect knowledge necessitates that He inform (speak), and thus He must have eternal speech.

Core Idea
God's perfect knowledge implies informing; speech is necessary to convey His knowledge.

Al-Isfarayini offers a supplementary proof: the perfection of actions indicates God's knowledge, and knowledge requires informing. Since God can obligate, teach, and alert, He must have speech. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Premise: actions indicate knowledge
Read how al-Isfarayini says that the perfection and precision of actions indicate that God is knowing.
This is the starting point.
2
The necessary link: knowledge and informing
Understand his claim that knowledge and informing are inseparable; you cannot know something without being able to inform about it.
This is the logical step.
3
Application to God
Observe that since God can obligate, define, inform, alert, guide, and teach, He must have speech. Thus He is a speaker.
This concludes the proof.

Source Text

يعلم شيئا ولا يخبر عنه فإن الخبر والعلم يتلازمان، فلا يتصور وجود أحدهما دون الثاني، ومن لا خبر عنده عن معلومه لا يمكنه أن يخبر غيره عنه، ومن المعلوم أن الباري يصح منه التكليف والتعريف والإخبار والتنبيه والإرشاد والتعليم، فوجب أن يكون له كلام وقول يكلف ويعرف ويخبر وينبه بذلك، فإذا ثبتت هذه الدلائل كونه متكلما. فنقول: إما أن يقال: هو متكلم لنفسه أو متكلم بكلام، ثم إن كان متكلما بكلام، فإما أن يكون كلامه قديما أو حادثا، وإن كان حادثا، فإما أن يحدث في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في محل، ولا قائل بكونه متكلما لنفسه من المعتزلة وغيرهم؛ إذ لا يعقل أزلا وأبدا، ولا قائل بكلام يخلقه لا في محل؛ لأن في نفي المحل نفي الاختصاص، وفيه إبطال التفرقة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يحدثه في ذاته كما صارت إليه الكرامية فقد سبق الرد عليهم، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل كما صار إليه المعتزلة فقد خالف قضية العقل؛ فإن الكلام لو قام بمحل لكان المحل متصفا به دون غيره من الفاعل وغيره كما لو تحرك متحرك بحركة يخلقها الله لم يرجع أخص وصفها إلى الفاعل، وكذلك سائر الأعراض، فبقي أنه متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي يختص به قياما ووصفا، وذلك ما أثبتناه.
The twelfth principle: Concerning that the Creator is a speaker with eternal speech. Since we have not found in the Islamic community anyone who disagrees with us that the Creator, exalted is He, is a speaker with speech, we have presented this issue even though the custom is to present the latter issue first, and only the philosophers, the Sabians, and the deniers of prophethood have disagreed with us in this, and the methods of the theologians of Islam differ. So the method of the Ash'aris is that they said: Reason indicates that the Creator, exalted is He, is living, and it is proper for the living to speak, command, and forbid, just as it is proper for him to know, have power, will, hear, and see. So if He were not described with speech, it would lead to His being described with its opposite, which is muteness, speechlessness, and incapacity, which are deficiencies, and He is exalted above them. That which verifies this method is their statement: It has been established by the evidence of reason that He is a king obeyed, and it is a rule of kingship that there be from him command and prohibition. Just as the oscillation of creation in the types of changes, events, and possibilities indicated that the Creator, exalted is He, is powerful and knowing, so the oscillation of creation in the types of command and prohibition indicates the command and prohibition of the Creator. And just as in His kingdom His decree runs, so upon His servants His obligation runs, and just as He disposes of necessary existents by compulsion and subjugation, He disposes of voluntary existents by obligation and definition. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, may God have mercy on him, followed another path, saying: Actions, by their perfection and precision, indicate that He, exalted is He, is knowing, and it is impossible that He knows something but does not inform about it, for informing and knowledge are inseparable, so it is inconceivable to have one without the other, and one who has no information about his known object cannot inform others about it. And it is known that obligation, definition, informing, alerting, guidance, and teaching are proper for the Creator, so it is necessary that He have speech and a word by which He obligates, defines, informs, and alerts. So when these proofs are established, He is a speaker. Then we say: Either it is said that He is a speaker in Himself or a speaker by speech. Then if He is a speaker by speech, either His speech is eternal or originated. And if it is originated, either it comes to be in His essence, or in a locus, or not in a locus. There is no proponent among the Mu'tazila and others that He is a speaker in Himself, for it is not intellectually conceivable eternally and ever. And there is no proponent of speech that He creates not in a locus, because negating the locus negates specification, and in it is the invalidation of the distinction between what subsists in itself and what does not subsist in itself. And whoever says that He is a speaker by speech that originates in His essence, as the Karramiyya held, the refutation against them has already preceded. And whoever says that He is a speaker by speech that He creates in a locus, as the Mu'tazila held, has contradicted the dictate of reason; for if speech were to subsist in a locus, the locus would be described with it to the exclusion of the agent and others, just as if a moving thing moves by a motion that God creates, its most specific description does not revert to the agent, and similarly for all accidents. So it remains that He is a speaker by eternal speech that He specifically owns in subsistence and description, and that is what we have established.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification aimed at establishing that God's speech is eternal. It begins by stating the topic: the twelfth principle is that God is a speaker with eternal speech. The author notes that only philosophers, Sabians, and deniers of prophethood dispute this. The Ash'ari method is then presented: reason shows God is living, and speech is a necessary concomitant of life; without it, God would have defects like muteness. This is reinforced by the 'king' argument: God is a king obeyed, and kingship implies command and prohibition; just as the variety in creation points to God's power and knowledge, the variety of commands and prohibitions points to His command and prohibition. Then Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini offers a supplementary proof: perfect actions indicate God's knowledge, and knowledge entails informing; since God can obligate, inform, etc., He must have speech. After these proofs, the author classifies possible positions: either God is a speaker in Himself (which is inconceivable) or by speech. If by speech, it is either eternal or originated. If originated, it either inheres in God's essence (Karramiyya, already refuted), in a locus (Mu'tazila, refuted because the locus would be described, not God), or nowhere (invalid because it negates specification). Thus, only the Ash'ari position remains: God is a speaker by eternal speech that subsists in His essence. The argument is structured as a disjunctive syllogism, eliminating all alternatives.

Key Terms
كلام
عالم
خبر
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Isfarayini, why does God's knowledge necessitate speech?
A) Because knowledge is an accident.
B) Because informing is inseparable from knowledge.
C) Because God needs to command.
D) Because actions are imperfect.

Key Takeaway
God's perfect knowledge requires that He inform, hence He must have speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's perfect knowledge necessarily compels Him to inform you through His speech, then what does it say about your neglect of the Qur'an? Will you open it today and let His words pierce your heart?
?
You claim to believe God knows everything about you—yet do you live as if you are accountable to the One who has informed you? When will you stop hiding from His speech and let it reshape your life?
?
Suppose you stood before God tomorrow and He asked: 'I informed you through My speech, but you did not listen. Why?' How would you answer? What will you change today so that answer is not silence?

The Disjunctive Syllogism: Establishing Eternal Speech
Ash'ari disjunctive syllogism to establish the eternity of God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris use a disjunctive syllogism to eliminate all alternatives and conclude that God's speech is eternal and subsisting in His essence.

Core Idea
By systematically eliminating each false view, only the Ash'ari position remains: God speaks with eternal speech.

After proving that God speaks, the text presents a classification of possible positions: either God is a speaker in Himself or by speech; if by speech, it is eternal or originated; if originated, it inheres in God's essence, or in a locus, or nowhere. Each alternative is refuted, leaving only the Ash'ari conclusion. This lesson walks through the logical elimination.

Guided Path
1
First division: speaker in Himself vs. by speech
Understand the initial bifurcation. The text states that being a speaker in Himself is inconceivable, so it is rejected.
This eliminates one branch.
2
Second division: eternal vs. originated speech
If by speech, it is either eternal or originated. Now examine the options for originated speech.
The goal is to eliminate all originated possibilities.
3
Options for originated speech
Originated speech could be in God's essence (Karramiyya), in a locus (Mu'tazila), or nowhere. Each is refuted.
Pay attention to the refutations.
4
Conclusion: only eternal speech remains
After eliminating all alternatives, only the Ash'ari position remains: God has eternal speech subsisting in His essence.
This is the final conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية عشرة في كون الباري متكلما بكلام أزلي. ولما لم نجد في الملة الإسلامية من يخالفنا في كون الباري تعالى متكلما بكلام قدمنا هذه المسألة وإن جرت العادة بتقديم المسألة الأخيرة، ولم يخالفنا في ذلك إلا الفلاسفة والصابية ومنكرو النبوات وطرق متكلمي الإسلام تختلف. فطريق الأشعرية أن قالوا: دل العقل على كون الباري تعالى حيا، والحي منه أن يتكلم ويأمر وينهي كما يصح أن يعلم ويقدر ويريد ويسمع ويبصر، فلو لم يتصف بالكلام أدى إلى أن يكون متصفا بضده وهو الخرس والعي والحصر وهي نقائص ويتعالى عنها. والذي يحقق هذه الطريقة قولهم: قد ثبت بدليل العقل أنه ملك مطاع ومن حكم الملك أن يكون منه أمر ونهي. كما دل تردد الخلق في صنوف التغايير والحوادث والجائزات على كون الباري تعالى قادرا عالما، دل تردد الخلق في صنوف الأمر والنهي على أمر الباري ونهيه. وكما جرى في ملكه تقديره، جرى على عباده تكليفه، وكما تصرف في الموجودات الجبرية جبرا وقهرا تصرف في الموجودات الاختيارية تكليفا وتعريفا. وقد سلك الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفراييني رحمه الله منهاجا آخر، فقال: دلت الأفعال بإتقانها وإحكامها على أنه تعالى عالم، ويستحيل أن يعلم شيئا ولا يخبر عنه فإن الخبر والعلم يتلازمان، فلا يتصور وجود أحدهما دون الثاني، ومن لا خبر عنده عن معلومه لا يمكنه أن يخبر غيره عنه، ومن المعلوم أن الباري يصح منه التكليف والتعريف والإخبار والتنبيه والإرشاد والتعليم، فوجب أن يكون له كلام وقول يكلف ويعرف ويخبر وينبه بذلك، فإذا ثبتت هذه الدلائل كونه متكلما. فنقول: إما أن يقال: هو متكلم لنفسه أو متكلم بكلام، ثم إن كان متكلما بكلام، فإما أن يكون كلامه قديما أو حادثا، وإن كان حادثا، فإما أن يحدث في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في محل، ولا قائل بكونه متكلما لنفسه من المعتزلة وغيرهم؛ إذ لا يعقل أزلا وأبدا، ولا قائل بكلام يخلقه لا في محل؛ لأن في نفي المحل نفي الاختصاص، وفيه إبطال التفرقة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يحدثه في ذاته كما صارت إليه الكرامية فقد سبق الرد عليهم، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل كما صار إليه المعتزلة فقد خالف قضية العقل؛ فإن الكلام لو قام بمحل لكان المحل متصفا به دون غيره من الفاعل وغيره كما لو تحرك متحرك بحركة يخلقها الله لم يرجع أخص وصفها إلى الفاعل، وكذلك سائر الأعراض، فبقي أنه متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي يختص به قياما ووصفا، وذلك ما أثبتناه.
The twelfth principle: Concerning that the Creator is a speaker with eternal speech. Since we have not found in the Islamic community anyone who disagrees with us that the Creator, exalted is He, is a speaker with speech, we have presented this issue even though the custom is to present the latter issue first, and only the philosophers, the Sabians, and the deniers of prophethood have disagreed with us in this, and the methods of the theologians of Islam differ. So the method of the Ash'aris is that they said: Reason indicates that the Creator, exalted is He, is living, and it is proper for the living to speak, command, and forbid, just as it is proper for him to know, have power, will, hear, and see. Then if He is a speaker by speech, either His speech is eternal or originated. And if it is originated, either it comes to be in His essence, or in a locus, or not in a locus. Refuting the Philosophers' Objection on God's Speech
Objection and counter-refutation regarding God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection against the Ash'ari proof for God's speech and the author's counter-refutation that turns the argument back on them.

Core Idea
The philosophers' objection is a mirror that reflects back on them.

This passage presents the challenge of philosophers and Sabians who claim that the Ash'ari argument for God's speech is merely an analogical inference from the visible world, lacking rational proof. The author responds by turning the tables: if they dismiss the Ash'ari proof as analogical, then their own defense against a similar refutation would be equally analogical, exposing the inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers claim that the Ash'ari proof is a mere claim without proof except analogy to the witnessed world.
Notice they say 'dawa mujarrada' (mere claim) and 'istirah ila al-shahid' (comfort from the witnessed).
2
Identify the author's strategy
The author says he will bring a refutation (naqd) of their principle, not to disprove the Ash'ari proof but to show inconsistency.
3
See the tu quoque
The author argues that the opponents' excuse (i'tidhar) against the refutation is the same as the Ash'ari's excuse against the objection, meaning the criticism applies to themselves.
This is a logical counter: if analogy is invalid, they cannot use it either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة والصابية: قولهم الحي يصح منه الاتصاف بالكلام؛ لأنه لو لم يتصف به لاتصف بضده، وكثيرا ما يطردون هذا الدليل في سائر الصفات وهي دعوى مجردة لا برهان عليها إلا الاسترواح إلى الشاهد، ونحن نورد عليكم نقضا لهذه القاعدة حتى يتبين أن اعتذاركم عن النقض اعتذارنا عن هذه.
The philosophers and Sabians said: Their statement 'the living, it is proper for him to be described with speech because if he were not described with it, he would be described with its opposite' is often applied by them to the rest of the attributes, and it is a mere claim without proof except seeking comfort from the witnessed. And we bring against you a refutation of this principle until it becomes clear that your excuse against the refutation is our excuse against this.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers and Sabians against the Ash'ari proof from life. They argue that the Ash'ari reasoning—that a living being must have speech otherwise it would have the opposite defect—is an analogical argument based on the witnessed world (shahid) without a rational proof. They claim it is a mere appeal to the sensible. The author then responds by stating that they will bring a counter-refutation (naqd) against this principle, showing that the opponents' own excuse (i'tidhar) against such a refutation is exactly the same as the Ash'ari's excuse against the objection. In other words, if the opponents dismiss the Ash'ari proof as merely analogical, then their own response to a similar counter-argument would be equally analogical, thus undercutting their objection. The author seems to be pointing out a tu quoque fallacy: the philosophers' criticism applies to themselves.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
صابية
شاهد
دعوى مجردة
نقض
اعتذار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proof for God's speech is purely analogical and thus invalid.
Correction: The author does not defend the proof here; instead he shows that the opponents' criticism is self-defeating, implying that if analogy is insufficient, their own method fails too.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of the philosophers against the Ash'ari proof for God's speech?
A) That the proof is based on scripture.
B) That the proof is a mere claim without proof except analogical appeal to the witnessed world.
C) That the proof proves God's speech is created.
D) That the proof is too complex.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection that the Ash'ari proof is merely analogical fails because their own defense against a similar refutation would be equally analogical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is it possible that your certainty is based on something less than rational proof? If so, will you honestly examine it, or will you defend it with the same excuses you criticize in others?
