Proof that God is a Speaker with Eternal Speech
The author proves God is mutakallim and refutes alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must have eternal speech and how the author refutes alternative views through logical classification.

Core Idea
God's speech must be eternal because originated speech contradicts reason and divine attributes.

In this passage, the author takes us through a rigorous logical classification of possible views on God's speech. He begins by establishing that God must be a speaker because He commands and informs. Then he systematically eliminates every alternative until only the eternal speech view remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the premise
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author starts by linking God's actions (commanding, informing) to the necessity of speech.
Focus on the phrase 'فوجب أن يكون له كلام وقول' – 'Therefore, He must have speech and a word.'
2
Trace the classification tree
Identify the author's branching logic: speaker in Himself vs. by speech; eternal vs. originated; originated in essence, locus, or no locus. Write down each branch.
The classification is exhaustive – every possible view is covered.
3
Understand why alternatives are rejected
For each rejected branch, note the author's argument: speaker in Himself is incoherent; originated speech not in a locus is impossible; originated in essence (Karramiyya) already refuted; originated in locus (Mu'tazila) contradicts reason because the locus would be described by speech, not the agent.
The key insight: if speech subsists in a locus, it cannot be attributed to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخبر عنه فإن الخبر والعلم يتلازمان، فلا يتصور وجود أحدهما دون الثاني، ومن لا خبر عنده عن معلومه لا يمكنه أن يخبر غيره عنه، ومن المعلوم أن الباري يصح منه التكليف والتعريف والإخبار والتنبيه والإرشاد والتعليم، فوجب أن يكون له كلام وقول يكلف ويعرف ويخير وينبه بذلك، فإذا ثبتت هذه الدلائل كونه متكلما. فنقول: إما أن يقال: هو متكلم لنفسه أو متكلم بكلام، ثم إن كان متكلما بكلام، فإما أن يكون كلامه قديما أو حادثا، وإن كان حادثا، فإما أن يحدث في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في محل، ولا قائل بكونه متكلما لنفسه من المعتزلة وغيرهم؛ إذ لو يعم تعقله أزلا وأبدًا، ولا قائل بكلام يخلقه لا في محل؛ لأن في نفي المحل نفي الاختصاص، وفيه إبطال التفرقة بين ما يقوم بنفسه وبين ما لا يقوم بنفسه، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يحدثه في ذاته كما صارت إليه الكرامية فقد سبق الرد عليهم، ومن قال: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل كما صار إليه المعتزلة فقد خالف قضية العقل؛ فإن الكلام لو قام بمحل لكان المحل متصفا به دون غيره من الفاعل وغيره كما لو تحرك متحرك بحركة يخلقها الله لم يرجع أخص وصفها إلى الفاعل، وكذلك سائر الأعراض، فبقي أنه متكلم بكلام قديم أزلي يختص به قياما ووصفا، وذلك ما [أثبتناه].
And He does not inform about it, for information and knowledge are inseparable; it is inconceivable that one exists without the other. And one who has no information about what he knows cannot inform another about it. And it is known that the Creator can validly issue obligation, definition, information, notification, guidance, and teaching. Therefore, He must have speech and a word by which He obligates, defines, informs, and notifies. So when these proofs establish that He is a speaker, we say: either He is said to be a speaker in Himself, or a speaker by speech. Then if He is a speaker by speech, then His speech is either eternal or originated. If it is originated, then it is either originated in His essence, or in a locus, or not in a locus. There is no proponent of Him being a speaker in Himself among the Mu'tazila and others, for that would imply comprehension from eternity to eternity. And there is no proponent of speech created not in a locus, because denying a locus denies specification, and that invalidates the distinction between what subsists by itself and what does not. And whoever says He is a speaker by speech originated in His essence, as the Karramiyya held, has already been refuted. And whoever says He is a speaker by speech created in a locus, as the Mu'tazila held, has contradicted reason; for if speech were to subsist in a locus, then the locus would be described by it to the exclusion of the agent and others, just as if a mover moves with a movement created by God, its specific description would not refer back to the agent—and similarly for all accidents. So it remains that He is a speaker by eternal, pre-eternal speech that pertains to Him in subsistence and description, and that is what we have established.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is a speaker (mutakallim) with an eternal speech (kalam qadim). The author begins by establishing the necessity of speech for God based on the fact that He commands, informs, and teaches. Since He does these things, He must have speech. Then the author classifies the possible positions: (1) God is a speaker in Himself (i.e., His essence is speaking), (2) He is a speaker by speech. The latter is then subdivided: either the speech is eternal or originated; if originated, it is either in His essence, in a locus (like a body), or not in a locus. The author dismisses each option: no one holds that He is a speaker in Himself; originated speech not in a locus is impossible because it would negate specification and distinction between self-subsisting and accidental attributes; originated speech in His essence is the view of the Karramiyya, already refuted; originated speech in a locus is the view of the Mu'tazila, but this is irrational because if speech subsists in a locus, the description belongs to that locus, not to God, just as a movement created by God in a body does not make the mover 'moving' by that movement. Therefore, the only remaining option is that God is a speaker by eternal, pre-eternal speech that subsists in His essence and is described by it.

Key Terms
كلام قديم
متكلم
ذات
محل
أعراض
معتزلة
كرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is created, like human speech, and occurs in a locus (e.g., a body or a created object).
Correction: If speech were created in a locus, the locus would be described by it, not God. Moreover, originated speech cannot be eternal, but God's attributes must be eternal. Therefore, God's speech must be eternal and subsist in His essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for God to have originated speech created in a locus?
A) Because that would make the locus the speaker, not God.
B) Because originated speech cannot be eternal.
C) Because the Karramiyya already refuted it.
D) Because no one claims it.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is eternal and subsists in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and uncreated, what does that imply about your relationship with the Quran? Is it merely a historical document, or is it the very speech of the Eternal? How does that change how you approach reading it today?
?
The author argues that speech must belong to the one who speaks, not to a locus. Reflect: In your life, whose voice are you listening to? Are you allowing created 'speech' (trends, opinions, desires) to shape your actions, or are you submitting to the eternal speech of your Creator? What specific step will you take today to prioritize listening to His speech over the noise of the world?
?
The proof eliminates all alternatives until only eternal speech remains. Are you as thorough in defending the truth of God's attributes in your own mind? Have you ever let a doubt about the Quran's eternal nature linger? What will you do to solidify your conviction?

Refutation of the Analogy Objection to Divine Speech
The author refutes the philosophers by showing inconsistency in their analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' claim that the proof for God's speech relies on an invalid analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
If analogical reasoning proves too much, it proves nothing.

The philosophers and Sabians objected to the argument for God's speech, claiming it merely appealed to analogies from the witnessed world. In this passage, the author turns their own logic against them, showing that the same reasoning would equally apply to other attributes they reject. This exposes the inconsistency in their objection and defends the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the repetition of 'shahid' (witnessed) and 'ghaib' (unseen).
2
Identify the objection and counter-argument
The philosophers claim the proof for speech is merely analogical. The author counters by showing the same reasoning would prove sensory attributes (smell, taste, touch) for God, which they reject.
The author uses the five senses as a test case for consistency.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why analogical reasoning from the witnessed world is limited. The author explains that conditions differ, so not all attributes transfer.
Think about the distinction between perfection in the witnessed vs. unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة والصائية: قولهم الحي يصح منه الاتصاف بالكلام لأنه لو لم يتصف به لاتصف بضده، وكثيرا ما يطردون هذا الدليل في سائر الصفات وهي دعوى مجردة لا برهان عليها إلا الاسترواح إلى الشاهد، ونحن نورد عليكم نقضا لهذه القاعدة حتى يتبين أن اعتذاركم عن النقض اعتذارنا عن الدعوى. وذلك أن الحي يصح منه الاتصاف بالحواس الخمس، ثم الثلث منها كالشم والذوق واللمس يصح في الشاهد ويصح وصفه بها والاتصاف بها، كما يصح منه الاتصاف بالسمع والبصر والكلام، ثم لم يجز طرد ذلك كله في الغائب، ولا يقال: إنه لو لم يتصف بها كان موصوفا بضدها، بل يجب أن يقال: يتعالى الحق عنها وعن أضدادها، كذلك قولنا في السمع والبصر والكلام. واعتذاركم أن اتصال الأجرام بتلك الحواس شرط في الشاهد، كذلك اعتذارنا أن البنية واتصال الأجرام واصطكاكها شرط في الشاهد، فلا يجوز أن يوصف الحي بها ولا بضدها جميعا، وكثيرا ما يكون من الصفات كمالا في الشاهد ونقصانا في الغائب، فبقيت الحجة الأولى عرية عن البرهان.
The philosophers and Sabians said: Their statement that the living [being] can validly be described with speech, because if it were not described by it, it would be described by its opposite—and they often apply this argument to all attributes, but it is a mere claim with no proof except appealing to the witnessed. We present against you a refutation of this rule so that it becomes clear that your excuse against the refutation is our excuse against the claim. That is, the living being can validly be described with the five senses; then three of them—like smell, taste, and touch—are valid in the witnessed, and it is valid to describe Him with them and be described by them, just as it is valid to be described with hearing, sight, and speech. But then it is not permissible to apply all of that to the Unseen, and it is not said that if He were not described by them, He would be described by their opposites; rather it must be said: the Truth is exalted above them and above their opposites. Similarly our statement about hearing, sight, and speech. And your excuse that the contact of bodies with those senses is a condition in the witnessed—similarly our excuse that the physical structure and contact of bodies and their collision are conditions in the witnessed. So it is not permissible to describe the Living with them nor with all their opposites. And often attributes are perfection in the witnessed but deficiency in the Unseen. So the first argument remains devoid of proof.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by the philosophers and Sabians against the proof for God's speech. They argued that the proof from 'the living' (al-hayy) is merely analogical (qiyas al-shaahid) and not demonstrative, and they claimed they could disprove it by showing that it would lead to other conclusions they reject. The author responds by turning the argument back on them: the same analogical reasoning they criticize for speech can be applied to the five senses—hearing, sight, etc.—but they do not accept that God has those senses in the same way He has speech. Thus, if they reject the analogy for senses, they should also reject it for speech. The author points out that in the witnessed world, sensory attributes require physical contact and instruments, but in the Unseen, we do not attribute them to God. Similarly, speech in the witnessed requires a physical apparatus, so we do not apply that analogically to God; rather, we affirm that He is transcendent above both those attributes and their opposites. Thus, the philosophers' objection fails, and their original argument remains unproven.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
صابئة
شاهد
غائب
حواس
برهان
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is rejecting all analogical reasoning from the witnessed world.
Correction: The author is not rejecting analogical reasoning entirely, but showing that the philosophers' own use of it is inconsistent. The passage demonstrates the limits of analogy when conditions differ.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main counter-argument against the philosophers' objection?
A) The philosophers' own analogical reasoning for speech would also prove sensory attributes for God, which they deny.
B) The philosophers have no proof at all for their objection.
C) The philosophers are Sabians and should be ignored.
D) The philosophers accept that God has senses, so they are inconsistent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection fails because the analogical reasoning they reject for speech also applies to other attributes they accept, showing their inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about God's attributes, are you sometimes selective about which analogies you accept based on your own comfort or preconceived notions, rather than being consistent? In what area of your faith might you be guilty of this inconsistency?
?
The philosophers rejected sensory attributes for God because they required physical conditions—but accepted speech for God without the same scrutiny. Have you ever accepted a belief about God simply because it was familiar, while rejecting another equally scriptural belief because it felt 'too human'? Which one are you holding back from?
?
If your own reasoning about Allah's attributes were tested by the same standards you apply to others' arguments, would your beliefs stand or fall? Take one attribute you feel strongly about—say, God's mercy or justice—and honestly ask: is your conviction based on proof from revelation, or on an analogy that you might reject if applied elsewhere?

God's Command Is Actual, Not Verbal
The author argues that God's command and prohibition are not verbal but actual acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that God's command and prohibition are actual acts (amr fi'li) rather than verbal speech, and how this resolves theological debates about divine speech.

Core Idea
God's command is not spoken but created knowledge in the heart.

In this passage, the author responds to the common assumption that God's command must be verbal, like a human king. He accepts that God is a king with command, but argues that His command is actual (fi'li), meaning He creates necessary knowledge in the commanded person. This resolves theological disputes about how God's speech subsists. The author also uses a Quranic verse to support his view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied objection
The passage begins by conceding that God is a king obeyed with command and prohibition. What is the implied objection from theologians that the author is addressing?
Theologians assume command must be verbal because a king speaks commands.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between verbal command (amr qawli) and actual command (amr fi'li). How does he define actual command?
He says it is by creating necessary knowledge in the commanded person that the act is obligatory.
3
Examine the refuted alternatives
The author dismisses two other views: created speech in a locus (Mu'tazila) and eternal speech heard intermittently. Why are they unacceptable?
Created speech is not truly God's command; eternal intermittent speech is absurd.
4
See the Quranic support
The author cites verse 41:11 where God 'says' to the heavens and earth. How does he interpret this 'saying' as an actual act rather than verbal speech?
It refers to God's act of directing them to submit, not literal speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إن الرب تعالى ملك مطاع ذو أمر ونهي فمسلم، لكنكم معاشر المتكلمين أنتم منازعون في إثبات الكلام له هو أمر ونهي على وجه يتصف به إما قياما بذاته وإما قياما بغيره. فإنا نقول: هو ملك مطاع وله أمر ونهي لا على طريق قولي بل على طريق فعلي، وهو تعريف المأمور خبرا أن الفعل المأمور به واجب الإقدام عليه بأن يخلق له معرفة ضرورية أن الأمر كذلك. إما أن يتكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل، أو يتكلم أزلا وأبدا بكلام يسمع في حال ولا يسمع في حال فهو من أمحل المحال. وهذا لأن تصريفه تعالى جواهر الخلائق بالفعل على وجه ينقاد له طوعا وكرها لما خلقه له وقدره فيه نازل منزلة القول. ألستم تقرون في كتابكم: {ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى إِلَى السَّمَاءِ وَهِيَ دُخَانٌ فَقَالَ لَهَا وَلِلْأَرْضِ ائْتِيَا طَوْعًا أَوْ كَرْهًا قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَائِعِينَ}.
As for your statement: 'Indeed the Lord, exalted, is a king obeyed, possessing command and prohibition'—it is accepted. But you, the assembly of theologians, are disputing regarding the establishment of speech for Him that is command and prohibition in a way that He is described by it, either subsisting in His essence or subsisting in another. For we say: He is a king obeyed, and He has command and prohibition not in a verbal way but in an actual way: it is the definition of the commanded one by information that the commanded act is obligatory to undertake, by creating for him necessary knowledge that the command is so. Either He speaks with speech He creates in a locus, or He speaks from eternity to eternity with speech that is heard at one time and not heard at another—that is among the most absurd of absurdities. And this is because His, exalted, direction of the substances of creatures through action in a way that they submit to Him willingly or unwillingly due to what He created and destined in them takes the place of speech. Do you not acknowledge in your book: 'Then He turned to the heaven while it was smoke and said to it and to the earth: Come willingly or unwillingly. They said: We come obediently'?
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a response to an implied objection: that God must have verbal command and prohibition as a king. The author accepts that God is a king with command and prohibition, but he distinguishes between verbal command (qawli) and actual command (fi'li). He argues that the theologians' dispute is about how God is described by speech—whether it subsists in His essence or in another. The author's position is that God's command is not verbal; rather, it is actual: He creates necessary knowledge in the person that the act is obligatory. This is a form of 'definition by information' (ta'rif bi-khabar). He then dismisses the alternative: either God speaks with created speech in a locus (Mu'tazila), which fails because that speech would not be His command in a real way, or He speaks from eternity with speech that is sometimes heard and sometimes not—which is absurd because eternal speech cannot be intermittent. The author supports his view with the Quranic verse (41:11) where God 'says' to the heavens and earth 'Come willingly or unwillingly,' and they respond. This is interpreted not as verbal speech but as an act of creation that makes them submit. Thus, God's command is actual, not verbal.

Key Terms
أمر فعلي
أمر قولي
معرفة ضرورية
كلام
ملك مطاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command must be verbal speech because He is a king.
Correction: God's command is actual: He creates necessary knowledge in the commanded person, not verbal speech.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain God's command without verbal speech?
A) By creating necessary knowledge in the commanded person that the act is obligatory.
B) By speaking in a way that is neither eternal nor created.
C) By using angels to convey the command.
D) By writing in a preserved tablet.

Key Takeaway
God's command is not verbal but an act of creating necessary knowledge in the commanded person, supported by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's command is not a mere word but an act that creates necessary knowledge in your heart, when you know something is obligatory, do you submit immediately like the heavens and earth, or do you hesitate? What does that hesitation reveal about your faith in His authority?
?
The passage says God's direction of creatures 'takes the place of speech.' In what specific situation today has God directed you through an inner conviction or circumstance? Are you resisting because you expected a louder, more verbal command?
?
The author rejects the idea that God's speech is sometimes heard and sometimes not. How often do you only 'listen' for God when you want to hear something convenient? What would it mean to live as if His command is always present and urgent?
